The Universal Proposition: Conjunctive and Disjunctive Proofs in Usul al-Fiqh
Explaining the logical structure of proof and defining the subject of usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the qiyas iqtirani and qiyas istithnai forms for proving a fiqh ruling, and identify the subject matter of usul al-fiqh as the study of proofs and rulings in terms of their essential attributes.

Core Idea
A universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) underlies every fiqh ruling; usul al-fiqh studies proofs and rulings through their essential attributes.

In this passage, the sharh author demonstrates how a fiqh ruling is proven using logical syllogisms. He shows that all usul al-fiqh discussions revolve around a single universal proposition that serves as the major premise in conjunctive syllogism or the conditional in disjunctive syllogism. Understanding this structure is key to grasping how usul al-fiqh provides the framework for deriving furu' rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya)
Recognize that every fiqh ruling is established by a universal proposition that incorporates all necessary conditions (e.g., mukallaf, absence of preventing accidents). The qadiyya kulliyya is the major premise in qiyas iqtirani or the conditional in qiyas istithnai.
The universal proposition must integrate discussions on the act (fi'l), the mukallaf, and preventing accidents.
2
Understand the two forms of qiyas
Distinguish between qiyas iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism: minor premise + major premise) and qiyas istithnai (disjunctive syllogism: conditional universal + assertion of the antecedent). The passage provides exact formulations for both.
In qiyas iqtirani, the minor premise specifies the ruling, act, and mukallaf; the major premise states that any such ruling is established by the described qiyas.
3
Trace the components of the proof
For each form, identify the parts: the claim (matalub), the minor premise (sughra), the major premise (kubra), and the conclusion (natija). Note the specific qualifiers mentioned (e.g., 'this quality' refers to the type of ruling, act, mukallaf).
The major premise in iqtirani is: 'Every ruling described with these qualities – the described qiyas indicates its establishment – is established.'
4
Recognize the subject matter of usul al-fiqh
The passage concludes that all discussions of usul revert to this universal proposition. The subject matter is shari proofs and rulings: proofs are studied as establishers (muthbit) of rulings, and rulings as being established (thabit) from proofs. This defines the scope of the science.
Essential accidents (awāriḍ dhātiyya) of proofs are establishing rulings; of rulings, being established.

الاحکام ہیں یعنی بعض مباحث کا تعلق ادلہ سے ہے اور بعض کا احکام سے جو مباحث راجع الی الادلہ ہوتی ہیں ان کی ادلہ میں بحث کی جاتی ہے اور جو مباحث راجع الی الاحکام ہوتی نہیں ان کی احکام کے ضمن میں بحث ہوتی ہے فموضوع هذا العلم مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ گذشتہ تقریر سے ثابت ہو گیا کہ علم اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ شرعیہ اور احکام ہیں کیونکہ اس علم میں ادلہ شرعیہ کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جاتی ہے اور اولہ شرعیہ کے جاتی ہے لہذا اس علم کا موضوع ہوئے ادلہ واحکام
The gist is that the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) will only establish the ruling when all these discussions are known. Otherwise the ruling is not established. So the composition of the proof for establishing fiqh issues is as follows: This ruling is established – this is the claim. Then the proof is in the form of conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani): the minor premise is 'the ruling is established because it is a ruling with this quality'; 'this quality' refers to whether it is wajib, mandub, etc., and the act of the mukallaf with its types, and the mukallaf himself with his essential types, and the absence of preventing accidents. Then the major premise: 'Every ruling described with the mentioned qualities – the described qiyas indicates its establishment – is established.' The conclusion: 'This ruling is established.' If using disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithnai), the form is: 'This ruling is established because whenever a qiyas described with these qualities is found indicating a ruling described with these qualities, that ruling is established; but such a qiyas is found; therefore the ruling is established.' So it is known that all preceding discussions are included in this universal proposition. The author says: It is well known from our previous exposition that all issues of usul al-fiqh revert to this universal proposition and are related to it, whether the proposition serves as the major premise of conjunctive syllogism or as the universal concomitant of disjunctive syllogism. From this it is also established that usul al-fiqh discusses universal shari proofs and universal rulings: proofs from the aspect that they establish rulings, and rulings from the aspect that they are established from proofs. So the subject matter of this science is shari proofs and rulings, because in this science we discuss the essential accidents of shari proofs, which are establishing rulings, and the essential accidents of rulings, which are being established from proofs.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion from a sharh on usul al-fiqh. It clarifies how the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) functions in proving fiqh rulings. The author first explains that for a ruling to be established via a universal proposition, all related discussions (such as the condition of the mukallaf, presence of preventing accidents, etc.) must be known. He then lays out the logical structure of proof using qiyas iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism) and qiyas istithnai (disjunctive syllogism). In qiyas iqtirani, the minor premise specifies the ruling, act, and mukallaf with their conditions, and the major premise states that any ruling meeting these conditions is proven by the described qiyas. In qiyas istithnai, a conditional universal is used: whenever a qualified qiyas indicates a qualified ruling, that ruling is established; since such qiyas exists, the ruling is established. The passage concludes that all usul al-fiqh discussions revolve around this universal proposition, and the subject matter of usul is shari proofs and rulings, studied in terms of their essential attributes (establishing and being established).

Key Terms
قیاس اقترانی (qiyas iqtirani): A conjunctive syllogism consisting of a minor and major premise, used to prove a fiqh ruling.
قیاس استثنائی (qiyas istithnai): A disjunctive syllogism using a conditional universal (if A then B; A is true; therefore B is true).
قضیہ کلیہ (qadiyya kulliyya): The universal proposition that serves as a major premise or conditional in proofs of fiqh rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) in proving a fiqh ruling?
A) It is the minor premise specifying the act of the mukallaf.
B) It serves as the major premise in qiyas iqtirani or the conditional in qiyas istithnai.
C) It is the conclusion of the syllogism.
D) It refers to the preventing accidents that block the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The qadiyya kulliyya is the backbone of usul al-fiqh, structuring all proofs of fiqh rulings through qiyas iqtirani or istithnai, and defining the subject matter as shari proofs and rulings.

Defining the Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh
Definition of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh, showing how all issues revert to a universal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the subject matter (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh according to the Hanafi tradition and identify the role of the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya).

Core Idea
All usul al-fiqh issues revolve around a single universal proposition, and the discipline studies general evidences and rulings, not specific cases.

Usul al-fiqh is the science that provides the tools for deriving fiqh rulings. In this passage, the author clarifies that the subject of this science is not individual rulings or specific proofs, but rather general categories: the universal shari' evidences and universal rulings. Understanding this scope is essential for correctly applying usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author states that all issues of usul al-fiqh revert to a single universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya). Locate this claim in the passage.
This proposition may take two forms: a major premise in conjunctive qiyas or a universal correlation in exceptional qiyas.
2
Recognize the two forms of the universal proposition
Distinguish between the conjunctive syllogism form (e.g., 'every ruling of such kind indicates its proof by qiyas') and the exceptional syllogism form (e.g., 'whenever such proof is found, it indicates such ruling').
These forms are the frameworks within which usul arguments operate.
3
Understand what is studied in usul al-fiqh
The passage explains that usul al-fiqh discusses general shari' evidences (adilla shar'iyya kulliyya) and general rulings (ahkam kulliyya). Evidences are studied as establishers of rulings, and rulings as established from evidences.
It does not study specific rulings like the obligation of prayer; rather, it studies the general characteristics of evidences and rulings.
4
Grasp the essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya)
The essential accident of an evidence is that it establishes a ruling (ithbat hukm); the essential accident of a ruling is that it is established from an evidence (thubit min al-dalil). This perspective defines the scope of discussion.
These accidents are what make the subject matter of usul al-fiqh unique.
5
Conclude the subject (mawdu')
Thus, the subject of usul al-fiqh is the shari' evidences and rulings, considered in terms of their essential attributes. This aligns with the Hanafi tradition's systematic approach.
The subject is not the bare entities but their states and accidents.

Misk al-Malih 233 and commentary of al-Far[?] has much involvement and all these discussions are included in this universal proposition, and the near means (tawassul) also aims that this universal proposition, keeping these discussions and conditions in view, will lead to fiqh. Then if it is known that all issues of usul are referred back, the author says: From our previous exposition it is well established that all the issues of usul al-fiqh are referred to this universal proposition and are related to it, whether that universal proposition is the major premise of conjunctive syllogism like 'every ruling of such and such indicates its proof by qiyas', or it is the universal correlation of exception syllogism like 'whenever such proof is found it indicates such ruling, that ruling is established'. So from this it is also established that in usul al-fiqh, the general shari' evidences and general rulings are discussed. Evidences are discussed in terms that they are establishers of rulings, and rulings are discussed in terms that they are established from evidences. And some states and discussions refer to evidences and some discussions refer to rulings. So those discussions that refer to evidences are discussed under evidences, and those that refer to rulings are discussed under rulings. So the subject of this science: the author says that from the previous exposition it is established that the subject of the science of usul al-fiqh is the shari' evidences and rulings, because in this science the essential accidents of shari' evidences are discussed, and the essential accidents of shari' evidences are the establishment of rulings (ithbat hukm). Similarly, the essential accidents of rulings are discussed, and their essential accidents are being established from evidences. So from the perspective of establishing and being established, evidences and rulings are discussed. Hence the subject of this science is evidences and rulings.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the subject matter (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh. The author begins by stating that all the issues of usul al-fiqh revolve around a single universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya). This proposition can be the major premise of a conjunctive qiyas or the universal correlation of an exceptional qiyas. The key point is that usul al-fiqh does not deal with specific rulings or specific proofs; rather, it deals with generalities: general shari' evidences and general rulings. Evidences are studied as 'establishers' of rulings, and rulings are studied as 'established' from evidences. The subject of the science is therefore the shari' evidences and the rulings themselves, but not in their concrete particularity; rather, in terms of their essential attributes (a'rad dhatiyya). The essential attribute of an evidence is that it establishes a ruling; the essential attribute of a ruling is that it is established from an evidence. Hence the discussion is about the states (ahwal) of evidences and rulings, not the bare entities. This aligns with the Hanafi usul tradition's emphasis on systematic derivation.

Key Terms
قضیہ کلیہ (qadiyya kulliyya): The universal proposition to which all usul issues refer, serving as the major premise or the universal correlation in syllogisms
ادیلی شرعیہ کلیہ (adilla shar'iyya kulliyya): General, universal shari' evidences (as opposed to specific proofs for particular rulings)
احکام کلیہ (ahkam kulliyya): General rulings that apply to all instances, not specific case rulings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the subject (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh?
A) Specific fiqh rulings
B) The general shari' evidences and general rulings
C) Only the Quran and Sunnah
D) The personal opinions of mujtahids

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh, in the Hanafi tradition, studies general shari' evidences and general rulings, with all issues revolving around a universal proposition.

The Subject and Scope of Usul al-Fiqh: States of Evidences and Rulings
Taftazani resolves a textual ambiguity and defines key terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh and explain why it studies the a'rad dhatiyya (essential accidents) of evidences and rulings, not the entities themselves.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh studies the essential accidents of shar'i evidences and rulings, not their essences.

A common misreading of the definition of usul al-fiqh leads one to think it investigates the actual proofs and rulings. The commentator Taftazani resolves this by pointing to a widespread grammatical ellipsis: the phrase 'from the evidences and rulings' really means 'from the states of the evidences and rulings.' This lesson unpacks that refinement and defines the key term 'ard (accident) as used in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
Read the matn phrase 'investigation in this science is from the evidences and the rulings' and note why it might be misinterpreted as studying the essences of evidences and rulings.
The confusion arises because the subject of a science is not what is investigated; rather, its a'rad are.
2
Locate Taftazani's solution
Recognize that Taftazani invokes the principle of omitted genitive (mudaf mahzuf) to read 'states of the evidences and states of the rulings'.
Such omission is common in Arabic scholarly usage, so no confusion remains.
3
Define the subject of a science
Understand that the subject of any science is that thing whose essential accidents are investigated. Apply this to usul al-fiqh: its subject is the shar'i evidences and rulings considered as a category, but the investigation focuses on their a'rad dhatiyya.
Example: medicine studies the human body (subject) but investigates its health and disease (a'rad).
4
Dissect the definition of 'ard
Use the legal_definition_dissector to break down 'ard as something external to the essence of a thing and predicated on it. Note that this includes both essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya) and accidental accidents (a'rad ghariba).
The definition requires two conditions: being external to the essence and being predicated on the subject.

His statement: 'Know that in this science investigation is about the evidences' meaning about their states. The purpose of the commentator is to answer a question. The question is: The text's phrase 'you know that investigation in this science is from the evidences and the rulings' appears to mean that in usul al-fiqh, the very evidences and the very rulings are investigated, whereas this is incorrect because the evidences and rulings are the subject of usul al-fiqh. The principle is that in a science, the subject itself cannot be investigated; rather, its states are investigated. The commentator Taftazani answers that here a genitive is omitted, that is, 'about the states of the evidences and the states of the rulings', meaning that in usul al-fiqh the states of evidences are investigated. Such omission of the genitive is widespread in the usage of the people, so no problem. His statement: 'So the subject of this science' is intended to define the subject. The commentator Taftazani defines the subject: The subject of a science is that thing whose essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya) are investigated in that science. For example, the subject of medicine is the human body because in it the essential accidents of the human body are investigated. And the meaning of 'ard here is being defined: The definition of 'ard in an absolute sense, whether essential accident or accidental accident, is that it is external to the essence of a thing and is predicated on that thing. So in 'ard, two things are necessary: first, being external to the essence of the thing, second, being predicated on that thing. In short and simple words, 'ard is something...

Commentary
This passage responds to a potential confusion: the text states that usul al-fiqh investigates 'from the evidences and rulings', which might be misinterpreted as investigating the evidences and rulings themselves (i.e., their essences). The commentator clarifies that the omission of the word 'states' (ahwal) is common; the intended meaning is that usul al-fiqh investigates the states/accidents of the evidences and rulings. The subject (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh is defined as the shari' evidences and rulings in the sense that their essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya) are studied. An accident ('ard) is defined as something external to the essence of a thing that is predicated on it. This aligns with the Hanafi tradition's emphasis on the properties (ahwal) of the subject matter.

Key Terms
عوارض ذاتیہ (a'rad dhatiyya): Essential accidents that are necessary concomitants of a thing, but not part of its essence
عرض ('ard): An accident: something external to the essence of a thing and predicated on it

B) Because there is an omitted word 'states' (ahwal)
C) Because the evidences are not the subject of usul
D) Because the phrase is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh studies the essential accidents of shar'i evidences and rulings, not the entities themselves, and 'ard is defined as external to essence and predicated on the subject.

Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh: Ahwal al-Adilla
Clarifying that usul al-fiqh researches the states (ahwal) of adilla and ahkam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the subject (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh and its objects of study (adilla and ahkam), and apply the principle that a science studies the states (ahwal) of its subject.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh studies the states of evidence, not the evidence itself.

This passage from a commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih addresses a key methodological point: what exactly does usul al-fiqh study? The text clarifies that usul al-fiqh does not research the evidences (adilla) and rulings (ahkam) themselves, but rather their states (ahwal). This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope and methodology of the discipline. Additionally, the definition of usul al-fiqh is refined to account for the muqallid, who only reaches the issues (masail) of fiqh, not fiqh itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic statement
The matn states that usul al-fiqh researches from the evidences and rulings. This would imply it studies them directly, which violates the principle that a science studies the states of its subject, not the subject itself.
This is a common misinterpretation.
2
State the principle about subject and states
In any science, the subject (mawdu') is the entity itself, but the science investigates its states (ahwal). For example, medicine studies the human body's states of health and disease, not the body itself as a lump of tissue.
This is a logical principle in classifying sciences.
3
Recognize Taftazani's solution
Taftazani explains that there is a deleted modifier (mudaf mahdhuf): the phrase actually means 'from the states of the evidences and the states of the rulings'. This is common in Arabic expression.
The deletion is assumed to avoid repetition.
4
Apply the refinement to the definition
If 'what leads' (ma tawassala) is taken generally, it would include the muqallid, who follows a mujtahid. But fiqh requires deriving rulings from the four evidences, which the muqallid does not do. Therefore, the definition must be restricted to 'leading to the issues of fiqh' or we must say that usul al-fiqh is for mujtahids only.
This shows the precision needed in defining usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولا يقال إلى الفقه غرض شارح توضیح متن ہے ! اگر منتوصل کو عام قرار دیا جائے تو تعریف میں تھوڑی سی تبدیلی کرنی پڑے گی ما توصل بھا الی الفقہ کی بجائے الی مسائل الفقہ کہا جائے گا وجہ شارح بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ مقلد قواعد اصول فقہ کے ذریعہ مسائل فقہ تک تو پہنچ سکتا ہے لیکن نفس فقہ تک نہیں پہنچ سکتا کیونکہ فقہ احکام کو ادلہ اربعہ سے جاننے کا نام ہے اور مقلد کاعلم بالا حکام ادلہ اربعہ سے نہیں ہوگا بلکہ قول مجتہد سے ہوتا ہے البتہ اس کا توصل مسائل فقہ تک ہوتا ہے قوله علم انه يبحث في هذا العلم عن الأدلة يعنى احوالها غرض شارح جواب سوال ۔ سوال ! یہ ہوتا ہے کہ متن کی عبار تعلم انه يحث في هذا العلم من الادلة والا حکام سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ اصول فقہ میں نفس ادلہ اور نفس احکام سے بحث ہوتی ہے حالانکہ یہ غلط ہے کیونکہ ادلہ اور احکام اصول فقہ کا موضوع ہیں ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ نفس موضوع سے علم میں بحث نہیں کی جاسکتی بلکہ اس کے حالات سے بحث کی جاتی ہے۔ شارح تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ یہاں مضاف محذوف ہے ای عن احوال الا دلتہ واحوال الاحکام کہ اصول فقہ میں احوال ادلہ سے بحث کی جاتی ہے اس قسم کے مضاف کا حذف عبارت قوم میں شائع ذائع ہے فلا اشکال
His statement: 'And it is not said to fiqh' – the purpose of the commentator is to clarify the text. If 'what leads' is taken as general, then a small change must be made in the definition: instead of 'what leads to fiqh', it will be said 'to the issues of fiqh'. The commentator states the reason: the muqallid can reach the issues of fiqh through the rules of usul al-fiqh, but cannot reach fiqh itself, because fiqh is knowledge of rulings from the four evidences, and the muqallid's knowledge of rulings is not from the four evidences but from the saying of the mujtahid. However, he does reach the issues of fiqh. His statement: 'Know that in this science one researches from evidences' meaning their states – the purpose of the commentator is to answer the question. The question is: from the text of the matn 'Know that in this science one researches from the evidences and the rulings' it is understood that in usul al-fiqh one researches into the evidences themselves and the rulings themselves, whereas this is incorrect, because the evidences and rulings are the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. The principle is that in a science, one cannot research into the subject itself, but only into its states. The commentator Taftazani answers that here there is a deleted modifier, i.e., 'about the states of the evidences and the states of the rulings', so that usul al-fiqh researches into the states of the evidences. This type of deletion of a modifier is common in the expressions of the people, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition expansion. It addresses two points. First, the definition of usul al-fiqh: if 'what leads' (ma tawassala biha) is interpreted generally, it would include the muqallid, but the muqallid only reaches the issues (masail) of fiqh, not fiqh itself, because fiqh requires knowledge from the four evidences, which the muqallid does not have. So the definition must be refined to say 'to the issues of fiqh' if including the muqallid. Second, it clarifies that usul al-fiqh researches the states (ahwal) of the evidences and rulings, not the evidences and rulings themselves. Taftazani resolves this by positing a deleted modifier, a common practice in Arabic expression.

Key Terms
ما توصل به إلى الفقه (ma tawassala bihi ila al-fiqh): That by which one arrives at fiqh (definition of usul al-fiqh).
أحوال الأدلة (ahwal al-adilla): States of the evidences; the actual object of study in usul al-fiqh.
مضاف محذوف (mudaf mahdhuf): A deleted modifier (genitive construct omitted for brevity, common in Arabic).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) There is a deleted modifier (مضاف محذوف) implying 'states of'.
C) The matn is incorrect.
D) The subject is different.

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh is the study of the states of evidence, not the evidence itself.

Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh and the Badihi-Nazari Distinction
Clarifying what the science investigates and its subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject of Usul al-Fiqh as proofs and rulings, classify the subject of an issue according to the four possibilities, and explain why only Consensus and Qiyas require proof of their authority, unlike the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Usul al-Fiqh investigates essential accidents of its subject and only discusses non-self-evident proofs.

Before diving into specific rules, a Hanafi usuli must understand what this science is about. This passage from al-Taftazani provides the methodological foundations: the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is the proofs (adillah) and rulings (ahkam), and its issues examine their essential accidents. Moreover, not every proof receives the same treatment; some are self-evident (badihi) and need no proof of their authority, while others require rigorous argumentation. Grasping this distinction is key to understanding why scholars dedicated whole chapters to Consensus and Qiyas but not to the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject of the Science
Determine what Usul al-Fiqh investigates. According to the passage, the subject is both the proofs (adillah) insofar as they establish rulings, and the rulings (ahkam) insofar as they are established from proofs.
The subject is not the proofs alone or the rulings alone, but both in their relational aspect.
2
Understand the Four Relational Possibilities of Issues
The passage outlines four ways the subject of an issue (mas'alah) relates to the subject of the science: (1) identical to the subject, (2) a type of the subject, (3) an essential accident of the subject, (4) a type of an essential accident.
Example: 'The Book establishes rulings' has 'the Book' as the subject of the issue, which is identical to the subject of the science.
3
Recognize the Role of Essential Accidents
Each issue predicates an essential accident ('arad dhati) onto its subject. The predicate is something that necessarily inheres in the subject (e.g., establishing rulings is an essential accident of the Book).
This ensures the discussion stays within the boundaries of the science.
4
Differentiate Badihi from Nazari Issues
The passage explains that Usul al-Fiqh only tackles issues that are theoretical (nazari) and not self-evident (badihi). The Qur'an and Sunnah are accepted as proofs by necessity, so their status is not argued. Consensus and Qiyas, however, require proof.
This distinction explains why chapters on Ijma and Qiyas exist, but not separate chapters proving the authority of the Qur'an and Sunnah.
5
Apply the Distinction to a Sample Proposition
Take the proposition 'Ijma establishes a ruling.' Identify its subject (Ijma), its predicate (establishing), and note that Ijma is a type of proof (a type of the subject of the science). Then explain why this proposition is discussed: because the authority of Ijma is not self-evident.
This step consolidates the four possibilities and the badihi/nazari distinction.

معنی میں ہو کر عرض ذاتی ہے العام الذی خص عند البعض کیلئے اور العام الذي خص غنہ ( عام مخصوص منه البعض ) موضوع کے عرض ذاتی (مطلق عام ) کی ایک نوع ہے کیونکہ عام کی دو قسمیں ہیں مخصوص منہ البعض اور غیر وہ ثابت من ادلہ ہیں ۔ فان قلت فما بالهم غرض ایک اشکال کا جواب دینا مقصود ہے اشکال یہ ہے کہ ادلہ کل چار قسم میں کتاب سنت اجماع قیاس اصولی حضرات ان چار میں سے اجماع اور قیاس کو اپنی اصول فقہ میں داخل کر کے ان کے مثبت لا حکام ہونے کو ادلہ سے ثابت کرتے ہیں اور اصول فقہ کے مسائل میں سے شمار کرتے ہیں لیکن قرآن اور حدیث کے ثبت للاحکام ہونے کو اصول فقہ کے مسائل میں سے نہیں بناتے اس کی کیا وجہ ہے فرق کیوں ہے یا تو تمام کے مثبت للاحکام ہونے کو اصول فقہ میں درج کر کے ثابت کیا جانا چاہئے تھا یا پھر کسی کو بھی ذکر نہ کیا جا تا نیز یہ اشکال بھی لازم آئے گا کہ اجماع اور قیاس ادلہ اربعہ میں سے ہیں جو کہ اس فن کا موضوع ہیں اور ضابطہ یہ ہے موضوع کی ذات سے اس علم میں بحث نہیں کی جا سکتی تو اصولی حضرات اجماع اور قیاس کے اثبات لا حکام سے بحث کیوں کرتے ہیں یہ تو ضابطہ مسلمہ کے خلاف ہے جواب ! شارح تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ یہ فرق اس لیے ہو گیا کہ اصولی حضرات اپنی کتب میں ان مسائل سے بحث کرتے ہیں جو کسی اور نظری ہیں اور جو مسائل بد بہیہ ہیں ان سے اصولی حضرات بحث نہیں کرتے کیونکہ ان کو دلائل سے ثابت کرنے کی ضرورت ہی نہیں ہے چونکہ کتاب اللہ اور سنت رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم کا محبت اور مثبت لحکم ہونا منزلہ ہدبھی کے تھا بوجہ شہرت بین العلماء اور بوجہ متقرر (ثابت) فی علم الکلام ہونے کے اس لیے ان کو ثابت کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں تھی لیکن اجماع اور قیاس کا
And the intended meaning of investigation into essential accidents: the question arose that in the definition of the subject, the investigator seeks from its essential accidents; what is meant by this investigation? The commentator clarifies that the purpose of investigating essential accidents is that in Usul al-Fiqh, the issues mentioned will have essential accidents as their predicates. The question then becomes: these essential accidents will be predicates; but what will be the subject of the issue? The commentator answers that there are four possibilities for the subject of an issue: (1) Sometimes the subject of the issue is the subject of the science itself, e.g., 'The Book establishes the ruling definitively' – here 'establishing a ruling' is an essential accident, it is predicated on 'the Book', which is the subject of the proposition and also the subject of this science. (2) Sometimes the subject of the issue is a type of the subject of the science, e.g., 'The command indicates obligation' – 'indicates' meaning 'establishes', 'establishing obligation' is an essential accident predicated on 'the command', and 'the command' is a type of the Book of Allah. (3) Sometimes the subject of the issue is an essential accident of the subject of the science, and the predicate is also an essential accident, e.g., 'The general indicates certainty' – 'indicates' in the sense of 'establishes' becomes an essential accident for 'the general', and 'the general' itself is an essential accident for the Book of Allah. (4) Sometimes the subject of the issue is a type of the essential accident, e.g., 'The general from which a part has been specified indicates...' – 'indicates' in the sense of 'establishes' is an essential accident for 'the general from which a part has been specified', and 'the general from which a part has been specified' is a type of the essential accident (absolute general) because the general has two types: that from which a part is specified and that from which a part is not specified. Summary: Essential accidents will be the predicates of issues, and the subjects will have four forms. And all investigations of Usul al-Fiqh: The commentator Taftazani says that all investigations of Usul al-Fiqh are for the purpose of establishing essential accidents – for the proofs (adillah) insofar as they are establishers of rulings, and for the rulings (ahkam) insofar as they are established from proofs. That is, all the issues of this science have predicates that are either 'establishment' (ithbat) or 'establishment' (thubut) or things involved in them. Therefore, the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is both proofs and rulings: proofs as establishes of rulings, and rulings as established from proofs. If you say: What is the matter with them? The purpose is to answer an objection: The proofs are four types: Book, Sunnah, Consensus, Qiyas. The usuliyyun include Consensus and Qiyas in their Usul al-Fiqh and prove that they are establishers of rulings, and count them among the issues of Usul al-Fiqh. But they do not include the fact that the Qur'an and Hadith establish rulings among the issues of Usul al-Fiqh. What is the reason for this difference? Either the establishment of all should be included and proven, or none. Moreover, there is another objection: Consensus and Qiyas are among the four proofs which are the subject of this science, and the rule is that the essence of the subject cannot be discussed in the science. So why do the usuliyyun discuss the establishment of rulings by Consensus and Qiyas, contravening the established rule? The commentator Taftazani answers that this difference occurred because the usuliyyun discuss in their books only those issues that are opinion-based (nazari) and do not discuss issues that are evident (badihi) because they do not need to be proven by proofs. Since the fact that the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) are establishers of rulings is self-evident due to fame among scholars and their being established in 'ilm al-kalam, they did not need to be proven. But as for Consensus and Qiyas, they are not self-evident, so they are discussed.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage discusses the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh and the relation of its issues to the subject. The commentator first explains that the essential accidents discussed in the science serve as predicates in the propositions (masa'il). The subject of each proposition can be: (1) the subject of the science itself (like 'the Book' in 'the Book establishes rulings'), (2) a type of that subject (like 'command' which is a type of the Book), (3) an essential accident of the subject (like 'the general' which is an essential accident of the Book), or (4) a type of an essential accident (like 'the general from which a part is specified'). Thus the science investigates the essential accidents of its subject, namely the proofs and rulings. A key point: the science does not discuss every proof equally; it discusses only those whose positive character is not self-evident. The Qur'an and Sunnah are known to be establishers of rulings by necessity; hence they are not argued for. However, Consensus and Qiyas require proof that they are valid proofs, so they are included. This distinction shows the methodological maturity of Hanafi usul: it limits the scope of inquiry to what is non-evident and debatable.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject of a science; that which the science investigates its essential accidents.
عرض ذاتی ('arad dhati): In this passage, it refers to the predicates of the propositions of Usul al-Fiqh, which are essential accidents of the subject (proofs or rulings).
بدیہی (badihi): Self-evident; not requiring proof. The Qur'an and Sunnah's authority is considered self-evident in this science.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the subject of Usul al-Fiqh?
A) Only the proofs (adillah)
B) Only the rulings (ahkam)
C) Proofs insofar as they establish rulings, and rulings insofar as they are established from proofs
D) The essential accidents of proofs and rulings

Key Takeaway
Usul al-Fiqh investigates essential accidents of its subject (proofs and rulings) and only discusses non-self-evident proofs like Ijma and Qiyas, while accepting the Qur'an and Sunnah as self-evident.

Method of Learning
intermediate
10 min
Why Ijma and Qiyas Are Discussed in Usul al-Fiqh but Not Quran and Sunnah
Response to an objection about the subject of usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul scholars distinguish between badihi (self-evident) and nazari (theoretical) proofs, and apply this distinction to determine which proofs require discussion within usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Qur'an and Sunnah are self-evident proofs; Ijma and Qiyas require proof within usul.

This passage addresses a key methodological question: why do Usul al-Fiqh texts discuss the authority of Ijma and Qiyas but not that of the Qur'an and Sunnah? The answer lies in the distinction between self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari) propositions. The commentator Taftazani explains that the Qur'an and Sunnah are already established as proofs in 'ilm al-kalam, so they need no further proof in usul. Ijma and Qiyas, being theoretical, require their own justification. This lesson walks through Taftazani's reasoning and its implications for the scope of usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the subject of usul al-fiqh
The passage first defines the subject of usul as adillah and ahkam. Grasp that the mas'il of usul are about essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyya) of these subjects. The predicate of a mas'ala is an 'arad dhati, and it is either an affirmation or something related.
This sets up the framework: the science discusses qualities (accidents) of its subject, not the subject's essence.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states: If usul al-fiqh includes all four sources (Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) as its subject, why are only Ijma and Qiyas treated as mas'il to be proven? And if they are part of the subject, discussing their essence violates the rule that a science does not investigate its own subject's essence.
The objection has two parts: inconsistency and methodological violation.
3
Apply the badihi/nazari distinction
Taftazani resolves the first part by classifying matters as either self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). The authority of the Qur'an and Sunnah is badihi due to their fame and establishedness in kalam. Ijma and Qiyas are nazari and therefore require proof within usul.
This distinction determines whether a topic is discussed in usul or presupposed from another science.
4
Address the second objection (essence discussion)
Discussing the essence of Ijma and Qiyas (i.e., proving they are proofs) is a necessary prerequisite to know their states (ahkam). Hence, it is allowed out of necessity. Without knowing the essence, the states cannot be established.
This answers why the rule 'no discussion of essence' is suspended here.
5
Apply the distinction to other examples
The passage notes that shadhdh qira'at (unusual recitations) from the Book and khabar wahid (isolated reports) from the Sunnah are also nazari, so their proof status is discussed in usul. This reinforces the principle.
These examples show the breadth of the badihi/nazari principle beyond the four main sources.

The subject of this discipline also is (2) Sometimes the subject of a mas'ala is one of the types of the subject of the science, e.g., 'al-amr yufid al-wujub' – 'yufid' in the sense of 'yuthbit' – i.e., the affirmation of obligation is an 'arad dhati for al-amr, and al-amr is one of the types of the Book of Allah. (3) Sometimes the subject of a mas'ala is itself one of the essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyya) of the subject of the science, and the predicate is also an 'arad dhati, e.g., 'al-'amm yufid al-qat'i' – 'yufid' in the sense of 'yuthbit' – occurs as an 'arad dhati for al-'amm, and al-'amm is also an 'arad dhati for the Book of Allah. (4) Sometimes the subject of a mas'ala occurs as one of the types of essential accidents, e.g., 'al-'amm alladhi khussa 'anhu al-ba'd yufid al-qat'i...' etc. Summary: Essential accidents will be predicates of mas'il, and the subjects will have four forms. And all the investigations of usul al-fiqh – the commentator Taftazani says – all the investigations of usul al-fiqh are for establishing essential accidents for the adillah and for the ahkam, for the adillah in as much as they are affirmers of ahkam, and for the ahkam in as much as they are affirmed from the adillah. Meaning, all mas'il of this discipline have predicates that are either affirmations or something related to affirmation and affirmation. Therefore, the subject of usul al-fiqh is both adillah and ahkam – the former as affirmers of ahkam, and the latter as affirmed from adillah. 'Fa-in qulta: fa-ma baluhum?' The purpose is to answer an objection. The objection is that the adillah are four types: Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas. The usuliyyun include Ijma and Qiyas in their usul al-fiqh and prove that they are affirmers of ahkam from the adillah, and count them among the mas'il of usul al-fiqh. But they do not make the affirmation of ahkam of the Qur'an and Hadith part of the mas'il of usul al-fiqh. What is the reason for the difference? Either all should be included and proven, or none. Also, another objection arises: Ijma and Qiyas are among the four adillah, which are the subject of this discipline. The rule is that the essence of the subject cannot be discussed in the science. So why do the usuliyyun discuss the affirmation of ahkam of Ijma and Qiyas? That contradicts the established rule. Answer: The commentator Taftazani answers that this difference arose because the usuliyyun discuss in their books those mas'il that are theoretical (nazari), and they do not discuss mas'il that are self-evident (badihi), because they have no need to prove them with evidence. Since the affirmation of ahkam of the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is self-evident due to its fame among scholars and its establishedness in the science of kalam, there was no need to prove it. But the authoritativeness and affirmation of ahkam of Ijma and Qiyas was theoretical, so there was a need for evidence. Therefore, the usuliyyun made them mas'il of usul al-fiqh and discussed them. Similarly, the affirmation of ahkam of shadhdh qira'at from the Book and khabar wahid from the Sunnah is theoretical, so their affirmation of ahkam is proven in usul al-fiqh. Hence the difference. Also, this answers the second objection: discussing their essence is done out of necessity, because unless the knowledge of the essence is obtained, knowledge of the states is impossible.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh and a response to an objection. The author first clarifies that the predicate of a mas'ala in usul is an essential accident ('arad dhati). He gives examples: 'al-amr yufid al-wujub' – the predicate (affirmation of obligation) is an 'arad dhati for amr, and amr is a type of the Book of Allah. Similarly, 'al-'amm yufid al-qat'i' – the predicate (affirmation of certainty) is an 'arad dhati for the general term, and the general term is an 'arad dhati for the Book. Then he explains that the subject of usul al-fiqh includes both the adillah (as affirmers) and the ahkam (as affirmed). The main objection raised is: Why do Hanafi usul scholars discuss Ijma and Qiyas as proofs (proving they affirm ahkam) within usul al-fiqh, but do not discuss the Qur'an and Sunnah in the same way? And also, if Ijma and Qiyas are part of the subject, discussing their essence violates the rule that a science does not investigate its own subject. The commentator Taftazani answers by distinguishing between self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari) matters. The Qur'an and Sunnah are universally accepted as proofs due to their fame and established status in kalam, so they do not need proof within usul. Ijma and Qiyas, however, require proof to establish their authority. The same applies to unusual qira'at and khabar wahid. As for the second objection, discussion of the essence of Ijma and Qiyas is necessary to know their states (ahkam) regarding proof.

Key Terms
عرض ذاتی ('arad dhati): An essential accident that is inseparable from the subject and used as a predicate in the mas'il of the science.
نظری (nazari): A matter that is not self-evident and requires theoretical proof.
بدیھی (badihi): A self-evident matter that does not require proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafi usul scholars discuss Ijma and Qiyas as proofs within usul al-fiqh, but do not discuss the Qur'an and Sunnah in the same way?
A) Because the Qur'an and Sunnah are not considered proofs in the Hanafi school
B) Because the authority of the Qur'an and Sunnah is self-evident (badihi), while the authority of Ijma and Qiyas is theoretical (nazari)
C) Because Ijma and Qiyas are not actually part of usul al-fiqh
D) Because the Qur'an and Sunnah are only discussed in al-Mabsut, not in usul

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the authority of the Qur'an and Sunnah is presupposed as self-evident, while Ijma and Qiyas require explicit proof because they are theoretical.

Rulings as an Appended Subject in Usul al-Fiqh
Explaining that rulings and their accessories are appended subjects in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why rulings are discussed subordinately in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and identify the primary subject of the discipline.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, evidence is primary; rulings are discussed only as they relate to evidence.

This passage from al-Tawdih clarifies the scope of usul al-fiqh. The author states that the discussion of rulings is appended to the main topic of evidence. The commentator emphasizes that rulings are treated 'tabaʿan tabaʿan' — subordinately. Understanding this relationship is crucial for correctly framing the subject matter of usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary subject of usul al-fiqh
The matn states that the discussion is 'about what is established by these evidences'. This indicates that evidences (adillah) are the main subject.
The pronoun 'bi-hi' refers back to the discussion of evidences.
2
Recognize the appended nature of rulings
The phrase 'ويلحق به' (and appended to it) shows that rulings are added to the primary discussion.
3
Understand the emphasis 'تبعا تبعا'
The commentator repeats 'tabaʿan' to stress that rulings are doubly subordinate, not a co-primary subject.
4
Connect to the structure of usul texts
In Hanafi usul works, sections on rulings typically come after detailed discussions of evidence, reflecting this subordination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن توضیح
ويلحق به البحث عما يثبت بهذه الادلة وهو الحكم وعما يتعلق به
مقصد عبارت یہ ہے کہ اس علم میں جس طرح ادلہ اور اس کے متعلقات سے بحث ہوتی ہے اسی طرح جو چیز ادلہ سے ثابت ہوتی ہے یعنی احکام اور ان کے متعلقات ان سے بھی بحث ہوتی ہے لیکن تبعا تبعا۔ مصنف اپنی عبارت کی خود توضیح فرما رہے ہیں کہ ولحق بہ کی ضمیر مصدر
Matn al-Tawdih: 'And appended to it is the investigation of what is established by these evidences, namely the ruling, and of what pertains to it.' The meaning of the passage is that just as in this science the discussion concerns the evidences and their accessories, similarly that which is established by the evidences, i.e., rulings and their accessories, is also discussed, but subordinately, subordinately. The author himself elucidates his statement: the pronoun in 'bi-hi' refers to the masdar (source).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This is the start of a new section in the Tawdih, where the author states that the investigation of rulings and their accessories is appended to the main discussion of evidence. The commentator clarifies that 'bi-hi' refers back to the discussion of evidence. This means that the primary topic of usul al-fiqh is the evidences (Quran, Sunnah, consensus, analogy, etc.), and the rulings derived from them are discussed only secondarily. The phrase 'tabaʿan tabaʿan' (تبعا تبعا) emphasizes that the discussion of rulings is subordinate, not independent. This is a standard Hanafi position: usul al-fiqh primarily deals with the sources and methods of derivation, while the rulings themselves are the domain of furūʿ al-fiqh. However, because understanding the ruling is necessary to understand the evidence, a brief treatment of rulings is included.

Key Terms
الحکم (al-ḥukm): The legal ruling (taklīfī or waḍʿī) that is established by the evidence; the secondary subject of usul al-fiqh.
تبعا (tabaʿan): Subordinately; meaning that rulings are not the primary subject but are discussed in a subsidiary manner within usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of the discussion of rulings in usul al-fiqh according to this passage?
A) It is the primary subject
B) It is appended and discussed subordinately
C) It is not discussed at all
D) It is discussed only in furūʿ al-fiqh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul al-fiqh treats rulings as an appended subject; the primary subject is evidence.

Essential Attributes in Usul al-Fiqh: The Third Category
Classification of essential attributes in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of essential attributes ('awārid dhātiyya) and distinguish which are included in usul al-fiqh and which are excluded.

Core Idea
Only essential attributes that affect the subject or predicate are studied; grammatical and historical attributes are excluded.

The commentator on al-Tawdih clarifies that usul al-fiqh does not deal with all attributes of evidences or rulings. Attributes that have no bearing on legal derivation—like a word being triliteral or a proof being eternal—are outside the discipline. Only those that affect the status of being a subject or predicate are relevant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The commentator divides essential attributes into three types: those that are the subject itself, those that affect the predicate, and those that affect neither.
Only the first two are part of usul al-fiqh.
2
Examine examples of the third type
Examples include a proof being customary, eternal, or contingent; a word's grammatical properties like being triliteral, inflected, or case endings.
These have no legal effect.
3
Apply the rule
Given any attribute, decide if it falls into the included or excluded category.
Focus on relevance to legal derivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عوارض ذاتیہ کی تیسری قسم کے متعلق فرماتے ہیں کہ عوارض ذاتیہ کی تیسری قسم خواہ وہ ادلہ کے ساتھ ہو یا احکام کے ساتھ، یہ تم ان اصول فن سے علیحدہ ہے، اس کا اصول فقہ کے ساتھ کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے۔ اصول فقہ میں صرف پہلی دو قسموں سے بحث ہوگی، یعنی جو عوارض ذاتیہ خود موضوع بنتے ہیں یا ان کو محمول میں دخل ہو، وہ عوارض ذاتیہ جو نہ خود موضوع میں نہ ان کو محمول سے عنہا گمان کوئی دخیل ہے وہ اصول فقہ کے مسائل و مباحث سے خارج ہیں۔ مثال: مثل کسی دلیل کا عادت ہونا، قدیم ہونا، حادث ہونا، یا اس کے مفردات کا ثلاثی مجرد، رباعی، معرب مبنی ہونا، منصرف غیر منصرف ہونا، مرفوع منصوب مجرور ہونا، یہ سب ایسے عوارض ذاتیہ ہیں جن کا اثبات اور ثبوت میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے۔ وهذا كما أن النجار... 

متن توضیح: ويلحق به البحث عما يثبت بهذه الادلة وهو الحكم وعما يتعلق به 
شرح: مصنف اپنی عبارت کی خود توضیح فرما رہے ہیں کہ ولحق بہ کی ضمیر مصدر بحث کی طرف راجع ہے جو کہ گذشتہ عبارت فیحت سے ماخوذ و مفہوم ہے۔ واعلم ان قوله ويلحق به يحتمل امرین: (۱) لحوق بمعنی بعد، یہ معنی مجازی ہے۔ اس معنی کے مطابق مقصد عبارت یہ ہوگا کہ اصول فقہ میں احکام کی بحث ادلہ کے احوال سے بحث کرنے کے بعد ہوگی، اولہ کی بحث پہلے ہوگی کیونکہ احکام ان پر موقوف ہیں۔ اس معنی کے مطابق یہ ثابت ہوگا کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ بھی ہیں اور احکام بھی، ادلہ کے مباحث پہلے ذکر کیے جائیں گے اور احکام کے مباحث بعد میں ذکر کیے جائیں گے جیسا کہ مذہب حق ہے۔ (۲) لحوق بمعنی التبع، یہ معنی حقیقی ہے۔ اس معنی کے مطابق مقصد عبارت یہ ہوگا کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع فقط ادلہ ہیں اور اصول فقہ میں ادلہ کے عوارض اور احوال سے ہی اصالہ بحث کی جاتی ہے اور احکام سے جو بحث کی جاتی ہے وہ تبعا ہوتی ہے۔ مصنف فرماتے ہیں احتمال اول صحیح ہے کہ مباحث احکام بھی اس فن کے مباحث میں داخل ہیں اور ادلہ اور احکام دونوں کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے اصالة بحث ہوتی ہے اور دونوں ہی اس علم کا موضوع ہیں کیونکہ اصول فقہ سے مقصود معرفت فقہ ہی ہے اور فقہ نام ہے احکام کو ادلہ سے جاننے کا اور احکام کو ادلہ سے جاننا قواعد اصول فقہ کے مطابق ہوگا لہذا احکام بھی اصالةً اصول فقہ میں داخل ہو جائیں گے اور ان کا موضوع بنیں گے۔
[Commentary:] Regarding the third type of essential attributes: whether they pertain to evidences or rulings, this type is separate from the principles of this discipline; it has no connection to usul al-fiqh. In usul al-fiqh, only the first two types are discussed: those essential attributes that themselves become subjects or have involvement in the predicate. Those essential attributes that neither become subjects themselves nor have any involvement in the predicate are excluded from the issues and discussions of usul al-fiqh. Example: like a proof being customary, eternal, contingent; or its parts being triliteral, quadriliteral, inflected, built; or its being munsarif or ghayr munsarif; raised, accusative, genitive — all such essential attributes have no effect on proof and substantiation. This is like the carpenter...

Text of al-Tawdih: 'And attached to it is the research about what is proven by these evidences, which is the ruling, and about what pertains to it.'
Commentary: The author himself clarifies his phrase, saying that the pronoun in 'yalhaqu bihi' refers to the source of 'research' derived from the previous phrase 'fihi'. Know that his statement 'yalhaqu bihi' admits two possibilities: (1) Attachment in the sense of subsequence, which is a metaphorical meaning. According to this, the meaning of the phrase is that in usul al-fiqh, the discussion of rulings comes after the discussion of the states of evidences; the discussion of evidences comes first because rulings depend on them. This proves that the subject of usul al-fiqh is both evidences and rulings, with evidences discussed first and rulings later, as is the correct doctrine. (2) Attachment in the sense of following, which is the literal meaning. According to this, the meaning is that the subject of usul al-fiqh is only evidences; in usul al-fiqh, only the attributes and states of evidences are discussed primarily, and the discussion of rulings is subsidiary. The author states that the first possibility is correct: the discussions of rulings are included in the discussions of this discipline, and both evidences and rulings are discussed with their essential attributes as the primary subject, and both are the subject of this knowledge because the purpose of usul al-fiqh is to gain knowledge of fiqh, and fiqh is the knowledge of rulings from evidences, and knowing rulings from evidences happens according to the rules of usul al-fiqh. Therefore, rulings are also primarily included in usul al-fiqh and become its subject.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Tawdih and al-Talwih discusses the scope of usul al-fiqh. The commentator first clarifies the third type of essential attributes ('awārid dhātiyya) as those that neither serve as the subject (mawḍū') of a ruling nor affect the predicate (maḥmūl) in the discipline; these are excluded from usul al-fiqh. Examples include grammatical properties of words or historical attributes—they have no bearing on legal derivation. Then the commentator turns to the matn's phrase 'wa yalḥaqu bihi al-baḥth' (and attached to it is the research). The pronoun 'bihi' refers to the research on evidences. Two interpretations arise: (1) 'attachment' meaning subsequence (ba'd), where rulings are discussed after evidences; (2) 'attachment' meaning subsidiary (taba'), where rulings are only secondary. The Hanafi school prefers the first, holding that both evidences and rulings are primary subjects of usul al-fiqh, because the goal is to know rulings—they are not merely appended. This reflects the Hanafi view that usul al-fiqh includes the study of rulings themselves, not just evidences.

Key Terms
عوارض ذاتية ('awārid dhātiyya): Essential attributes that attach to the subjects (evidences or rulings) of usul al-fiqh; only those that affect the status of being a subject or predicate are studied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of an essential attribute that is excluded from usul al-fiqh?
A) A proof being customary.
B) A proof being decisive (qat'i).
C) A ruling being obligatory (wajib).
D) A proof being general (amm).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits the scope of usul al-fiqh to essential attributes that influence legal derivation.

The Status of Rulings in Usul al-Fiqh: Subsequence or Subsidiary?
Analysing the inclusion of rulings in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two interpretations of 'yalhaqu bihi al-bahth' and state the preferred Hanafi position on whether rulings are a primary subject of usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Rulings are a primary subject of usul al-fiqh, not merely subsidiary; they are discussed after evidences but with full status.

The matn of al-Tawdih states that the research on rulings is 'attached' (yalhaqu) to the research on evidences. The commentator examines two possible meanings: attachment as subsequence (ba'd) or as subsidiary (taba'). The Hanafi school adopts the first, making rulings a co-primary subject alongside evidences, because the purpose of usul is to know rulings from evidences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its ambiguity
The phrase 'wa yalḥaqu bihi al-baḥth' can mean 'attached to it is the research' where 'it' refers to evidences. The word 'luhūq' has two senses.
This ambiguity drives the discussion.
2
Examine the first meaning: subsequence
If luhūq means ba'd (after), then rulings are discussed after evidences. Both are subjects, but in sequence.
This is the preferred Hanafi view.
3
Examine the second meaning: subsidiary
If luhūq means taba' (following), then rulings are only secondary topics, with evidences as the only primary subject.
This would reduce the scope.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The commentator chooses the first meaning because usul aims at knowing rulings, so they must be a primary subject.
This is consistent with the definition of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عوارض ذاتیہ کی تیسری قسم کے متعلق فرماتے ہیں کہ عوارض ذاتیہ کی تیسری قسم خواہ وہ ادلہ کے ساتھ ہو یا احکام کے ساتھ، یہ تم ان اصول فن سے علیحدہ ہے، اس کا اصول فقہ کے ساتھ کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے۔ اصول فقہ میں صرف پہلی دو قسموں سے بحث ہوگی، یعنی جو عوارض ذاتیہ خود موضوع بنتے ہیں یا ان کو محمول میں دخل ہو، وہ عوارض ذاتیہ جو نہ خود موضوع میں نہ ان کو محمول سے عنہا گمان کوئی دخیل ہے وہ اصول فقہ کے مسائل و مباحث سے خارج ہیں۔ مثال: مثل کسی دلیل کا عادت ہونا، قدیم ہونا، حادث ہونا، یا اس کے مفردات کا ثلاثی مجرد، رباعی، معرب مبنی ہونا، منصرف غیر منصرف ہونا، مرفوع منصوب مجرور ہونا، یہ سب ایسے عوارض ذاتیہ ہیں جن کا اثبات اور ثبوت میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے۔ وهذا كما أن النجار...

8 min
Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh: Evidences and Rulings
Explaining the two meanings of 'yulhaq bihi' and determining the subject of usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two meanings of 'yulhaq bihi' and explain why the Hanafi school includes both evidences and rulings in the subject of usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The subject of usul al-fiqh includes both evidences and rulings, as rulings are the goal of derivation.

This passage from Misk al-Malīḥ clarifies a foundational issue: what does usul al-fiqh actually study? By analyzing the phrase 'wa yulḥaqu bihi', the commentator unpacks two possible interpretations—one metaphorical and one real—and argues for including rulings alongside evidences. This determines the scope of the entire discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguity in 'yulḥaqu bihi'
Recognize that the pronoun 'bihi' refers to 'al-ḥukm' and that 'yulḥaqu' can mean either 'after' (metaphorical) or 'following' (real). This ambiguity affects whether rulings are a primary or secondary topic.
2
Contrast the two interpretations
First interpretation: rulings are discussed after evidences, so both are part of the subject. Second interpretation: only evidences are primary; rulings are annexed secondarily. Trace the consequences for the subject definition.
The first is metaphorical, the second real.
3
Analyze the author's preferred view
The author states the first probability is correct, arguing that since fiqh is knowledge of rulings from evidences, both must be primary. This is supported by analogy with logic.
4
Recognize the practical implication
Understanding the subject determines whether discussions of ahkam (like whether a ruling is shar'i or not) are included in usul al-fiqh proper. The Hanafi view includes them.
This is the mufta bihi position.

This discussion refers back to what was derived and understood from the previous phrase 'fīhā' because the derived term indicates the source, and in 'ammā thabata' the word 'mā' has a deleted construct preceding it, i.e., 'an aḥwāl mā thabata' because states are what is asked about. Thus it was said: the pronoun in 'taʿalluq bihī' refers back to 'al-ḥukm', and by 'mutaʿallaq bi al-ḥukm' is meant the subject of the ruling and the person subject to it. Know that his statement 'wa yulḥaqu bihi' admits of two possibilities. Since 'yulḥaqu bihi' had two probabilities, the author explains them. The summary is that 'mulḥaq' is from 'luḥūq', and here 'luḥūq' can have two meanings: (1) 'luḥūq' in the sense of 'after', which is a metaphorical meaning. According to this, the purpose of the phrase would be that in uṣūl al-fiqh, the discussion of rulings occurs after the discussion of the states of evidences; the first discussion comes earlier because rulings depend on them. According to this meaning, it would be confirmed that the subject of uṣūl al-fiqh is both evidences and rulings. The discussions of evidences will be mentioned first and the discussions of rulings later, as is the correct school. (2) 'luḥūq' in the sense of 'following' (al-tabaʿ), which is the real meaning. According to this, the purpose of the phrase would be that the subject of uṣūl al-fiqh is only evidences. In uṣūl al-fiqh, discussion is primarily about the accidents and states of evidences, and the discussion of rulings is secondary. Because by 'uṣūl al-fiqh' is meant the evidences of fiqh. Then in these evidences, the aspect of being proof for rulings was considered. Therefore, originally 'uṣūl al-fiqh' is called so for these evidences alone, and their discussion is the main one. Discussions arising from rulings and their appurtenances are outside uṣūl al-fiqh; that is, they are not called uṣūl al-fiqh. But since those issues were few and rare, they were mentioned as subordinate and annexed to the issues of uṣūl al-fiqh. An example is given: the subject of logic is concepts and judgments, in that they lead to conception and assent. In logic, two things are discussed: the means leading (al-muwaṣṣil) and the end (al-muwaṣṣal ilayhi). But the main discussion is about the means leading, and most important issues also refer to the states of the means leading, so that becomes the subject of logic. The issues of the end are not few and rare, so their discussion is secondary, not primary, and they are not included in the subject. For example, the discussion of whether essences accept definition is about the end. Similarly, here in uṣūl al-fiqh, the discussion of evidences is primary and of rulings is secondary. According to this meaning, the subject of uṣūl al-fiqh is only evidences; rulings are outside the subject. This is why some books of uṣūl do not mention discussions of rulings and deem them external to the discussions of this discipline. But the author says the first probability is correct: discussions of rulings are also included in the discussions of this discipline, and both evidences and rulings are discussed primarily regarding their essential accidents, and both are the subject of this science. Because the purpose of uṣūl al-fiqh is knowledge of fiqh, and fiqh is the knowledge of rulings from evidences. And knowing rulings from evidences occurs according to the principles of uṣūl al-fiqh. Therefore, rulings also become primarily included in uṣūl al-fiqh and form its subject, because in uṣūl al-fiqh
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the statement 'wa yulḥaqu bihi' from the matn of Al-Tawḍīḥ. The commentator, Misk al-Malīḥ, explains that the word 'yulḥaqu' has two possible meanings: one metaphorical (ba'd = after) and one real (taba' = following). The first meaning implies that rulings are discussed after evidences, making both part of the subject of usul al-fiqh. The second implies that only evidences are the primary subject, with rulings discussed only secondarily because rulings are derived from evidences. The author favors the first interpretation, arguing that since the goal of usul al-fiqh is to derive rulings from evidences, both are essential and both are discussed primarily. He supports this by analogy with logic, where the means (muwaṣṣil) is the main subject but the end (muwaṣṣal ilayhi) is also discussed primarily when it is not rare; here, rulings are not rare, so they are included. This is a Hanafi perspective; the author aligns with the view that the subject of usul al-fiqh includes both adillah and ahkam.

Key Terms
ملحق (mulḥaq): Something annexed or joined; here, the annexation of rulings to evidences in the discussion of usul al-fiqh.
لحوق (luḥūq): Joining; with two possibilities: (1) after (ba'd) — metaphorical; (2) following (taba') — real.
اصالتہ (aṣālah): Primarily; as the main subject of discussion.
تبعا (tabaʿan): Secondarily; as a subordinate topic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred interpretation in this passage, what is the subject matter of usul al-fiqh?
A) Only evidences (adillah)
B) Only rulings (ahkam)
C) Both evidences and rulings
D) Neither evidences nor rulings

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that both evidences and rulings form the subject of usul al-fiqh, as rulings are the goal of derivation.

Why Proofs Precede Rulings: Two Rational Arguments
Providing two rational arguments for the sequential arrangement of topics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rational arguments for ordering proofs before rulings in usul al-fiqh and explain the concept of taqaddum dhati.

Core Idea
Proofs logically precede rulings: inherent priority dictates the order in usul.

You may have noticed that classical usul al-fiqh texts always discuss proofs (adilla) before rulings (ahkam). This is not accidental. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind this arrangement as explained by Sharh al-Talwih. Understanding this logical priority helps you appreciate the coherent structure of Hanafi legal methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'after'
The matn says rulings are mentioned 'after' proofs. The commentary notes two possible meanings: temporal sequence or logical priority. The author intends logical priority.
The term 'luhuq' here means posteriority in the order of presentation.
2
Understand the first argument: inherent priority (taqaddum dhati)
Proofs have inherent priority over rulings because rulings are derived from proofs. Since proofs are the means and rulings the end, the means logically come first. This is not temporal but essential priority.
Taqaddum dhati is a logical priority, like the priority of cause over effect.
3
Understand the second argument: importance
In usul al-fiqh, the discussions of proofs are considered the primary subject (al-mabahith al-awwal), while rulings are secondary. Because proofs are the foundation and more fundamental, they are placed first.
This is a pedagogical choice: establish the tools before applying them.
4
Connect to the author's arrangement
Both arguments justify why the author of the matn placed discussions of proofs before discussions of rulings. The first argument is logical, the second is pedagogical.
This ordering is standard in Hanafi usul works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح تلويح قوله ان يذكر مباحث الحكم بعد مباحث الادلة مصنف نے متن میں لحوق کے دو معنی ذکر کئے تھے پہلا معنی بعد والا اس کا مقصد یہ تھا کہ احکام کی بحث ادلہ کے مباحث کے بعد ہوگی اس پر شارح تفتازانی دو دلیلیں بیان کر رہے ہیں پہلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ادلہ کو حکم پر تقدم ذاتی حاصل ہے لہذا اس کو وضع میں بھی مقدم ہونا چاہئے دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ اصول فقہ میں مباحث ادلہ اہم نہیں بنسبت حکم کے اس لئے مباحث اولہ کو پہلے اور مباحث حکم کو بعد میں ذکر کیا جاتا ہے
Commentary on Talwih: His saying: 'He mentioned the discussions of rulings after the discussions of proofs.' The author mentioned two meanings of 'after'. The first meaning is 'later', and its purpose was that the discussion of rulings will come after the discussions of proofs. On this, Sharh Taftazani presents two proofs: First proof: Proofs have inherent priority over rulings, so they should also be presented first in the composition. Second reason: In usul al-fiqh, the discussions of proofs are not more important compared to rulings, so the primary discussions are mentioned first and the discussions of rulings are mentioned later.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's statement about the order of topics. Sharh Taftazani provides two rational arguments. The first is based on the inherent priority of proofs over rulings: proofs are the means to establish rulings, so they are logically prior. The second reason is that in usul al-fiqh, the discussions of proofs are the main subject, while rulings are secondary. Both reasons justify the author's arrangement.

Key Terms
تقدم ذاتی (taqaddum dhati): Inherent priority in essence; logical priority independent of time.
لحوق (luhuq): Following after; posteriority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first argument for ordering proofs before rulings?
A) Proofs are more lengthy
B) Proofs have inherent priority over rulings
C) Rulings are not as important
D) The author preferred it that way

Key Takeaway
Proofs precede rulings in usul al-fiqh because of their inherent logical priority and their foundational role in the discipline.

The Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh: Do Rulings Belong?
Presenting a dispute over the subject matter of usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions on the subject matter of usul al-fiqh and explain the Hanafi majority's proof for including rulings.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh investigates both the proofs and the rulings they establish, not just the proofs alone.

In this passage, the commentator Taftazani presents a dispute about the very definition of usul al-fiqh: what does it study? Al-Amidi restricts it to the four proofs, but the majority, including the Hanafi school, argue that rulings must also be part of the subject. Understanding this debate clarifies how the discipline's scope shapes its method.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the two positions: al-Amidi (only adillah) vs. majority (adillah + ahkam).
Al-Amidi is a Shafi'i scholar.
2
Analyze al-Amidi's proof
He says rulings are only a taswir (picture) needed for establishing or negating, not substantive issues.
Taswir is a necessary preliminary, not a subject of investigation.
3
Analyze the majority's proof
The commentator argues that discussions of how proofs affirm rulings involve both adillah and ahkam, so both must be subject matter.
The rational argument: some discussions revert to proofs, some to rulings.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The author and commentator declare the majority view rajih, rebutting the idea that adillah alone suffice.
Frequency/importance of proof discussions does not exclude rulings.
5
Identify the practical implication
This dispute affects categorizations in usul: whether to include topics like types of hukm as core subject matter.
Hanafi usul texts include discussions on ahkam, supporting the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لكن الصحيح الخ شارح تفتازانی اس عبارت میں موضوع اصول فقہ کے بارے میں اختلاف کو ذکر کر رہے ہیں (۱) علامہ آمدی شافعی صاحب الا حکام نے کہا ہے کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع فقط ادلہ اربعہ ہیں اور اصول فقہ میں احکام کے احوال سے بحث نہیں کی جائے گی بلکہ احکام کا صرف تصویر کافی ہے اور یہ تصور اس لیے ضروری ہے تاکہ حکم کا اثبات کیا جاسکے (اگر اپنے مذہب کے ہوں ) یا ان کی نفی کی جاسکے (اگر غیر کے مذہب کے ہوں) کیونکہ حکم کا اثبات بالدلیل یا فی بغیر تصور کے ناممکن ہے اس لیے تصور تو ضروری ہے البتہ اس کی تصدیق اور قضیہ کلیہ بنا کر اصول فقہ کا مسئلہ بنانا یہ کوئی ضروری نہیں ہے (۲) دوسرا مذ ہب یہ ہے کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ اربعہ اور احکام دونوں ہیں نہی مذہب جمہور ہے اور اس کو مصنف اور شارح نے راجح قرار دیا ہے شارح اس کے صحیح ہونے پر دلیل قائم کر رہے ہیں کہ جب ہم نے اولہ کی طرف رجوع کیا اور ان کو عام کیا تو ہم نے کل چار ادلہ حاصل کیے اور احکام میں رجوع کیا تو کل پانچ اقسام نکلے پھر ہم نے ان مباحث میں غور و فکر کیا جوادلہ کے مثبت للا حکام ہونے کی کیفیت کے ساتھ تعلق پکڑتے ہیں تو ہم نے دیکھا کہ بعض احوال اور مباحث تو ادلہ کی طرف راجع تھے اور بعض احوال احکام کی طرف راجع تھے جیسا کہ مصنف نے قضیہ کلیہ کی تحصیل کے ضمن میں اس کو مفصلا بیان فرمایا تھا تو جب مباحث متعلقہ بکیفیتہ اثبات ادلہ ملا حکام میں سے بعض مباحث کا تعلق ادلہ سے ہے اور بعض کا احکام سے ہے تو اصول فقہ کا موضوع بھی ادلہ اور احکام دونوں کو ہونا چاہئے تو ادلہ کو مقاصد سے اور احکام کو لواحق سے بنانا تحکم (حکم بلا دلیل ) اور سینہ زوری ہے زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ مباحث اولدا اکثر اور اہم ہیں اس بات کو تو ہم بھی تسلیم کرتے ہیں لیکن ادلہ کے مباحث کا اکثر اور اہم ہونا اس بات کا تقاضہ نہیں کرتا کہ وہ بالا صالۃ والاستقلال موضوع بن جائے اور احکام موضوع نہ ہوں لہذا صحیح قول یہ ہے کہ اولہ اور احکام دونوں موضوع ہیں البتہ ادلہ کے مباحث اکثر اور اہم ہیں۔
His saying: 'But the correct view...' The commentator Taftazani mentions in this passage the disagreement about the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. (1) Allamah Amadi Shafi'i, author of Al-Ihkam, said that the subject matter of usul al-fiqh is only the four proofs, and discussions of the states of rulings are not undertaken in usul al-fiqh; rather, only a picture (taswir) of rulings is sufficient, and this picture is necessary so that the ruling can be established (if from one's own madhhab) or negated (if from another madhhab), because establishing a ruling by proof or negating it without a conception is impossible. So conception is necessary, but to make it a universal proposition and a problem of usul al-fiqh is not necessary. (2) The second madhhab is that the subject matter of usul al-fiqh is both the four proofs and rulings. This is the majority madhhab, and the author and commentator have declared it rajih. The commentator establishes its correctness: When we referred to the first and generalized them, we obtained four proofs, and when we referred to rulings, we obtained five categories. Then we reflected on the discussions that relate to the quality of proofs being affirmative of rulings, and we saw that some circumstances and discussions refer back to proofs and some refer back to rulings, as the author explained in detail in the derivation of universal propositions. So when some discussions of the quality of proof-affirmation relate to proofs and some to rulings, then the subject matter of usul al-fiqh must include both proofs and rulings. To make proofs the maqasid and rulings the lawahiq is arbitrary and tyrannical. At most it can be said that the discussions of proofs are more frequent and important. We accept that, but the frequency and importance of proof discussions does not require that they alone be the subject matter and rulings not. So the correct statement is that both proofs and rulings are subject matter, but proof discussions are more frequent and important.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh about the scope of the discipline. Al-Amidi (Shafi'i) restricts the subject to the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) and considers rulings only as a necessary preliminary picture. The Hanafi majority, including the author and Taftazani, argue that rulings must also be part of the subject because many discussions in usul directly pertain to rulings. The commentator provides a rational proof: when analyzing the discussions regarding how proofs establish rulings, some aspects relate to proofs (e.g., conditions of proofs) and others relate to rulings (e.g., types of obligations). Hence both must be included. This view is accepted as the correct one.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): Subject matter of a discipline; what it investigates
ادلہ اربعہ (adillah arba'ah): The four proofs: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas
تصویر (taswir): Conception; a mental picture necessary for establishing or negating a ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the majority Hanafi view on the subject matter of usul al-fiqh?
A) Only the four proofs
B) Only rulings
C) Both proofs and rulings
D) Only qiyas

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of usul al-fiqh according to the Hanafi majority includes both the four proofs and the rulings they establish.

Method of Learning
