10 min
Conditions for a Single Science to Have Multiple Subjects
Usul discussion on conditions for multiple subjects in a science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three cases regarding whether a single science can have multiple subjects and apply the Hanafi condition of essential accidents arising from both related things.

Core Idea
Multiple subjects are allowed only when the subject involves a relation and its essential accidents arise from both related things.

Scholars debate whether a single science (ʿilm) can have multiple subjects (mawḍūʿāt). This passage presents a Hanafi investigation (taḥqīq) that refines the absolute claim, establishing that only under specific relational conditions can a science have more than one subject. The author illustrates with Usul al-Fiqh (multiple subjects) and logic (single subject), defending the principle that the unity of a science depends on the unity of its subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Recognize that the debate is about the possibility of a single science having multiple subjects. The author rejects the absolute claim and provides a conditional answer.
2
Understand the key concepts
Grasp the terms: mawḍūʿ (subject), ʿāriḍ dhātī (essential accident), iḍāfat (relation). The author uses these to analyze the structure of a science.
These terms are fundamental in Hanafi usul for defining the scope of a science.
3
Learn the three cases
The author distinguishes based on whether the subject involves a relation (iḍāfat) and, if so, whether the essential accidents arise from both related things or just one. Memorize the three scenarios.
Case 1: relation + accidents from both → multiple subjects allowed. Case 2: relation + accidents from one → single subject. Case 3: no relation → single subject.
4
Apply to examples
Analyze the given examples: Usul al-Fiqh (multiple subjects) and logic (single subject). Understand why each falls into its respective case.
Usul al-Fiqh: subject is relation between proofs and rulings; accidents arise from both. Logic: subject is conveyance of a concept; accidents arise only from the conveyed concept.
5
Evaluate the counterarguments
Examine the objections raised (e.g., the medicine example, the convention argument) and the author's responses. This sharpens your ability to defend the Hanafi position.
The author refutes the medicine example by clarifying that medicines are accidental, not essential to the subject.

سمجھ لیا جائے کہ ان دونوں میں تعدد موضوع جائز ہے
Misk al-Malih: Commentary on al-Tawdih and al-Talwih.
Two: one is the human body and the second is medicines, because in the science of medicine, the conditions of both the human body and medicines are discussed. It is proven that a single science can have multiple things as its subject. And this is not correct. The author is stating his investigation: In my view, to say absolutely that every science can have multiple subjects is not correct; rather, in some cases a single science can have multiple things as its subject, and in some cases it is not permissible. With 'al-tahqiq', the author is explaining its detail. The investigation is that the thing investigated (i.e., that thing whose states and essential accidents are discussed in the science, as another subject) is free from two categories: either the thing investigated is an attribution of one thing to another (i.e., the subject is something that has a relation and connection to another thing), or the subject is not an attribution of one thing to another. If the subject is an attribution of one thing to another, then there are two cases: either those accidents that are involved in the investigated thing arise from both related things, meaning some accidents, discussions, and states are related to one attribution (one related) and some to the other attribution (the other related); or all accidents and discussions are related only to one of the related things. If the accidents are related to both related things, then in this case a single science can have multiple subjects, and both related things will be the subject of the science. For example, Usul al-Fiqh: in it, the investigated thing is the attribution of one thing to another, because in it the establishment of proofs for rulings is discussed. So the establishment of proofs is an attribution and relation to the ruling; this is called the attribution of one thing to another. Then those accidents that are involved in this attribution (establishment) — some of them are related to the proofs, like generality, specificity, commonality, etc., and some are related to the rulings, like worship, punishment, etc. Hence, in this science, the conditions of both are discussed, and both will be the subject of this science: both the proofs and the rulings. If the investigated thing (subject) is an attribution of one thing to another, but the essential accidents involved in the investigated thing do not arise from both related things; rather, they are related only to one of the related things, then in this case the subject of the science is one; multiple subjects are not permissible. For example, the subject of logic is the conveyance of a concept to another concept. In it, the investigated thing is 'conveyance', which is attributed and related to 'another concept'. But the essential accidents related to the investigated thing — all of them are related to the conveyed concept, which is one of the two related things (as the author explained in the text). Therefore, the subject of logic is only one: the conveyed concept. Similarly, if the investigated thing is not an attribution of one thing to another, but rather the subject is singular, then too multiple subjects are not permissible; rather, the subject will be one. For example, the subject of fiqh is the action of the legally responsible person. In summary, there are total three cases: (1) The investigated thing is the attribution of one thing to another, and the accidents arise from both related things: in this case, multiple subjects are permissible. (2) The investigated thing is the attribution of one thing, and the accidents arise from one of the two related things: in this case, multiple subjects are not permissible. (3) The investigated thing is not an attribution: in this case also, multiple subjects are not permissible. Because the unity of the science, etc. — the author is presenting the proof for the non-multiplicity of subjects. The summary is that the unity and diversity of a science depend on the unity and diversity of its problems and objects of knowledge. If the objects of knowledge and problems are the same, then the science is one; if the objects of knowledge are different, then the sciences will be different. Then the unity and diversity of problems and objects of knowledge depend on the unity and diversity of the subject. If the subject is one, then objects of knowledge and problems are also one; and if the subject is different, then problems are also different. The rule is that whatever something depends on, the thing it depends on itself also depends on that something. Therefore, the unity and diversity of a science depend on the unity and diversity of its subject. Now, if a single science has multiple subjects, then the multiplicity and difference of subjects would require the multiplicity and difference of science, whereas we have already assumed it to be one science. On this basis, it is said that a single science has only one subject, not more than one, because difference of subject necessarily requires difference of science. 'Wa in urida' is a rebuttal of an objection: that a person could say that your statement that difference and multiplicity of subject causes multiplicity of science is not acceptable, because we can make a convention that a certain science is one; according to our convention, it is one science even if there is no common aspect among its problems and objects of knowledge, even if the subjects are multiple. Therefore, saying that multiplicity of subject necessarily requires multiplicity of science is incorrect. The author responded: If a person makes such a convention, it has no weight, because no one holds that without any common aspect the science can be considered one despite differences of problems and objects of knowledge. Hence this convention is not valid. 'Ala anna li kull' is a second proof of falsity: If this is accepted, then every person would have the right to make a convention, combining different sciences into one, because it is not necessary for those problems and objects of knowledge to be connected by a single aspect. Now if someone makes a convention that the science of fiqh and the science of geometry are one science, with their subjects being the action of the legally responsible person and magnitude, then you would have to accept his statement, because you have given everyone the freedom to make convention. 'Wa ma awrada min al-nazir' — the author is responding to the proof of the majority of investigators: that the example you gave of the science of medicine is not correct, because the subject of medicine is one: the human body. Medicines are not the subject of medicine; rather, they are among its essential accidents, because medicines are discussed insofar as some medicines make the body healthy and some make it ill. So these medicines are related to the conditions of the body. Hence they are among the essential accidents, not the subject. This proof is not correct. The author has given two examples in the investigation of multiple subjects: one is the subject of Usul al-Fiqh — the establishment of proofs for rulings; and the second is the subject of logic — the conveyance of a concept to a concept. From mentioning both together, it should not be understood that in both, multiple subjects are permissible.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Usul al-Fiqh discussion on whether a single science ('ilm') can have multiple subjects (mawdu'at). The author, commenting on al-Tawdih, presents his own investigation (tahqiq) rejecting an absolute claim that any science can have multiple subjects. He distinguishes three cases based on whether the subject-matter involves a relation (idafa) and whether its essential accidents arise from both related things or just one. Usul al-Fiqh is given as an example where multiple subjects are possible: its subject is 'establishing proofs for rulings' — a relation between proofs and rulings, with accidents from both. Logic is counterexample: its subject 'conveying a concept to another concept' yields accidents from only the conveyed concept, so one subject. The author then defends the principle that unity/diversity of science depends on unity/diversity of subject, arguing that mere convention cannot make different sciences one without a common aspect. He refutes an example from medicine, clarifying that substances are accidental, not essential, to the subject. This is pure Hanafi usul method, grounded in rational analysis (dalil 'aqli) and consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on rigorous definition and avoidance of arbitrary taxonomy.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The thing whose states and essential accidents are investigated in a science
عارض ذاتی (ʿāriḍ dhātī): Essential accident that necessarily belongs to the subject of a science
اضافت (iḍāfat): Relation or attribution of one thing to another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi investigation in this passage, which of the following is a condition for a single science to have multiple subjects?
A) The subject must be a relation (iḍāfat) and essential accidents must arise from both related things
B) The subject must be a relation and essential accidents must arise from only one related thing
C) The subject must be singular without any relation
D) Any science can have multiple subjects by convention

Key Takeaway
A single science can have multiple subjects only when its subject is a relation and its essential accidents arise from both related things; otherwise, the subject is one.

Method of Learning
intermediate

Conditions for Multiplicity of Subject Matter in a Science
Dispute on conditions for multiple subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a single science can have multiple subjects and evaluate the dispute between Sadr al-Shari'ah and Taftazani regarding the subject of logic.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of subject requires proportionality (tanāsub); without it, a single science cannot have multiple subjects.

This lesson examines a key epistemological principle in Hanafi uṣūl: can a single science have more than one subject matter? The author Ṣadr al-Sharīʿah presented a simple two-scenario rule, but the commentator Taftazānī refined it with a third scenario and additional conditions. Through this analysis, you will understand how rational arguments and examples from geometry and medicine shape the definition of a science's subject.

Guided Path
1
State the author's two-scenario rule
Identify the two cases from Ṣadr al-Sharīʿah: (1) if the investigated matter involves a relation (iḍāfah) between two things, multiplicity is allowed; (2) if no relation, multiplicity is not allowed.
This is the baseline position.
2
Identify Taftazānī's three-scenario refinement
Taftazānī introduces a third scenario: relation exists but accidents arise from only one relatum – in that case multiplicity is not allowed. He also adds a second condition: accidents must come from both relata.
This refines the author's first scenario.
3
Analyze the first objection to the author's premises
Taftazānī challenges the author's two premises linking subject difference, issue difference, and science difference. He distinguishes between mere multiplicity of issues (which does not necessitate different sciences) and lack of proportionality (which does).
This critique focuses on the meaning of 'ikhtilāf al-masā'il'.
4
Apply the proportionality condition using examples
See how geometry (subjects share an essential genus: quantity) and medicine (subjects share an accidental purpose: relation to health) justify single sciences despite multiple subjects.
Proportionality can be essential (dhātī) or accidental (ʿaraḍī).
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: the subject of logic is single (Taftazānī) or multiple (author). This affects how the science of logic is defined and its scope.
The dispute is analytical, not about validity of rulings.

(۳) اغذیه (۴) ادویه (۵) امزجه (۶) ارکان ، ارکان سے مراد اخلاط اربعہ ہیں (۱) سود اوی (۲) صفراوی (۳) بلغمی (۴) دموی یہ تمام اشیاء کثیرہ ایک عرضی میں شریک ہیں وہ ہے ان کا منسوب الی الصحہ ہونا جو کہ اس علم کی غرض وغایت ہے اس سے ثابت ہوا کہ اگر موضوعات متعدد ہوں اور آپس میں متناسب ہوں تو ایسا تعدد جائز ہے اور یہ بھی معلوم ہو گیا کہ جو حضرات تعدد موضوع کے قائل ہیں وہ بھی شرط لگاتے ہیں کہ ان موضوعات کا آپس میں تناسب ہو بغیر
The subject will be one; multiplicity of subject is not permissible. For example, the subject of logic is 'conveying a concept to a concept' – there is an addition between the two things (conveying to another concept), but the accidents that enter into the investigated matter originate only from one addendum, namely the conveying concept, and all refer to it; from the other addendum, the concept conveyed to, no accident originates, as the author explained. Therefore, the subject is only the conveying concept. This is the second scenario that the author did not mention in the text; the author made only two scenarios. (3) If the investigated matter has no addition at all, then multiplicity of subject is not permissible; rather, the subject is one, such as the subject of fiqh: the actions of the obligated person, obligation, prohibition, etc. This is the third scenario; here too multiplicity of subject is not permissible. The reason is that difference and multiplicity of subject necessitates difference of issues (masā'il) and multiplicity of known things; and multiplicity of known things necessitates difference and multiplicity of the science, because when the known things are multiple, the science too will necessarily be multiple. And by 'known things' is meant the issues (masā'il). So the conclusion is that difference of subject entails difference of science. When the subject is multiple, the science too will be multiple, whereas the science was assumed to be one. So it is proven that when the science is one, the subject too will be one; the subject cannot be multiple. Thus the claim is established. Benefit: According to the exposition of the commentator Taftazani, in terms of multiplicity and non-multiplicity of subject, there are [three] scenarios here, whereas according to the author's exposition, there are two divisions. (1) If the investigated matter involves an addition between two things, then multiplicity of subject is permissible, whether the accidents originate from both addenda or from one addendum. So according to the author's exposition, the subject of logic (īṣāl) is also an example of multiplicity of subject, whereas according to the commentator's exposition, it is not an example of multiplicity but rather of non-multiplicity. (2) The second division: when the investigated matter does not involve addition. Between the two expositions there are three differences: (1) The author's exposition is based on two divisions, Taftazani's on three. (2) According to the author's exposition, the subject of logic is also multiple, like the subject of uṣūl al-fiqh, contrary to Taftazani's exposition, because in this case the subject of logic is one, not multiple. (3) According to the author's exposition, multiplicity of subject is conditioned by one condition: that the subject be an addition between two things; whereas according to Taftazani's exposition, multiplicity is conditioned by two conditions: (i) addition, and (ii) that some of the accidents originate from one addendum and some from the other. There is something to consider here. From here, the commentator Taftazani, as is his habit, raises objections against the author. He raises three objections. First objection: The origin of the first objection is the two premises that the author mentioned in the text. First premise: difference of subject entails difference of issues, and difference of issues entails difference of science. Second premise: We ask: what do you mean by 'difference of issues'? If you mean multiplicity of issues, then we do not accept the second premise, because it is obvious and self-evident that a single science has many hundreds of thousands of issues, yet it is called one science. If you mean by multiplicity of issues the lack of proportionality among them – that if there are many issues and they have no mutual suitability, then such difference entails difference of science – then we accept the second premise, but we do not accept the first premise: that multiplicity and difference of subject entails difference of issues in the sense that if subjects are multiple and many, then the issues also become different and have no mutual suitability or connection. Rather, we say that multiplicity of subject has two scenarios: (1) The subjects are multiple and many, and these many subjects have no mutual proportionality or connection with each other. In this case, by consensus, multiplicity of subject entails multiplicity and difference of issues; when the subjects have no suitability, the issues will also have difference in the sense of lack of proportionality among issues. (2) The subjects are multiple and many, but they are all mutually proportionate and connected. In this case, we do not accept that multiplicity of subjects entails difference of issues (in the sense of lack of proportionality). Rather, in this case, it is permissible that multiple proportionate subjects exist but the science is one and its issues have no difference; rather, all issues are proportionate with each other, because the scholars have explicitly stated that multiple numerous things can be the subject of a single science provided they have proportionality with each other. Then proportionality has two scenarios: (1) All the multiple subjects share a common essential (jins, etc.), like the subjects of geometry: three things: line, surface, and a teaching body (length, width, depth). These three share an essential: quantity, which is continuous quantum stable in essence. 'Quantity' means magnitude, and it has two types: (i) discrete quantity, like numbers, (ii) continuous quantity. Continuous quantity has two types: (i) stable in essence, whose all parts can be collected in a single time, like line, teaching body, etc.; (ii) non-stable in essence, whose parts cannot be collected in a single time, like time itself. Now these three are multiple, but due to sharing a genus, they are mutually proportionate. Therefore, the science whose subject they are (geometry) is called one science. (2) The second scenario of proportionality is that all those multiple subjects share some accidental (ʿaraḍ), for example, the subject of medicine can be taken as multiple things: the human body, its parts, foods, medicines, temperaments, elements (the four humors: black bile, yellow bile, phlegm, blood). All these multiple things share an accidental: their relation to health, which is the purpose and goal of this science. From this it is proven that if subjects are multiple and mutually proportionate, such multiplicity is permissible. And it also became known that those who hold multiplicity of subject also stipulate that these subjects have proportionality with each other; without...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the rule concerning the multiplicity of subject matter (taʿaddud al-mawḍūʿ) in a single science, a key principle in the epistemology of the Islamic sciences (uṣūl al-fiqh, logic, etc.). The author (Ṣadr al-Sharīʿah) had presented two scenarios in his matn: (1) if the subject involves an idāfah (relation) between two things, multiplicity is allowed; (2) if there is no idāfah, it is not allowed. The commentator Taftazānī, in his Talwīḥ, refines this by proposing three scenarios. The passage begins by explaining the scenario where there is idāfah but accidents arise from only one relatum, such as in logic where the conveying concept (taṣawwur mūṣil) is the subject because accidents only come from it. This is Taftazānī's second scenario not mentioned by the author. Then it gives the third scenario: no idāfah at all (e.g., fiqh's subject of actions of the mukallaf). The passage then contrasts the two expositions: according to the author, the subject of logic is multiple (like uṣūl al-fiqh); according to Taftazānī, it is single. Taftazānī also adds a second condition: accidents must come from both relata. Next, Taftazānī raises three objections against the author. The first objection (detailed here) challenges the author's two premises: (1) difference of subject entails difference of issues, (2) difference of issues entails difference of science. Taftazānī critiques the first premise by distinguishing two meanings of 'difference of issues': mere multiplicity (which does not entail science difference) and lack of proportionality (which does). He argues that if the author means mere multiplicity, the second premise is false; if he means lack of proportionality, then the first premise is false when subjects are proportionate. Taftazānī then explains with examples from geometry (where subjects share a genus) and medicine (where subjects share an accidental purpose). The passage concludes by stating that all scholars agree that proportionality is a condition for allowing multiple subjects in a single science. This commentary is essential for understanding how Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh considers the structure of sciences and the conditions for a unified discipline.

Key Terms
تعدد الموضوع (taʿaddud al-mawḍūʿ): The possibility or permissibility of a single science having more than one subject matter.
اضافت (iḍāfah): A relational meaning between two things, such that the subject involves a relational aspect.
عوارض (ʿawāriḍ): Accidental attributes or problems that are investigated in a science; they are the subject matter of inquiry.
تناسب (tanāsub): Proportionality or mutual suitability among subject matters, which justifies them being under one science.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazānī's exposition, what additional condition is required for multiplicity of subject matter in addition to the presence of a relation (iḍāfah)?
A) The relation must be between more than two things.
B) Accidents must originate from both relata of the relation.
C) The subject must be a single entity without any relation.
D) The science must have only one problem (mas'alah).

Key Takeaway
The permissible multiplicity of subject matter in a science depends on proportionality (tanāsub) among subjects, either essential (shared genus) or accidental (shared purpose).

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
Multiple Subjects in One Science: The Condition of Relation (Tanāsub)
Refutation of objections against multiple subjects in a single science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when multiple subjects can constitute a single science in Hanafi usul and apply the condition of tanāsub to evaluate objections.

Core Idea
A single science can have multiple subjects only if related by common genus or accident — relation is the key.

This passage defends the principle that a single science can encompass multiple subject matters, provided they are connected by a shared genus (like quantity in geometry) or a shared accident (like relation to health in medicine). The author refutes three objections raised by Taftazani, clarifying that ‘difference in issues’ means lack of relation, not mere multiplicity. Understanding this rule is essential for grasping how Hanafi usul al-fiqh is defined as a unified discipline despite covering diverse topics like the four sources and ahkam.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and its condition
Identify the core claim: multiple subjects can form a single science only if they are related (tanāsub). Distinguish between two types of relation: by a common genus (dhati) or by a common accident (aradi).
The rule is from the base text being commented on; the condition of tanāsub is added by the commentator.
2
Apply the rule to the examples
Analyze the two examples given: geometry (line, surface, solid share the genus ‘quantity’) and medicine (body, foods, drugs, humors share the accident ‘relation to health’). Confirm why each forms a single science.
These examples show that relation can be intrinsic (dhati) or extrinsic (aradi) but must be relevant to the science's goals.
3
Identify Taftazani's three objections
List the objections: (1) ambiguity in ‘difference of issues’ (multiplicity vs. lack of relation); (2) the rule allows arbitrary combination (e.g., fiqh and geometry); (3) the author's own examples (usul al-fiqh and logic) contradict his claim by having multiple subjects.
These objections challenge the consistency and applicability of the rule.
4
Evaluate the refutation of each objection
Follow the responder's rebuttal: (1) clarifies ‘difference’ means lack of relation, not multiplicity; (2) the condition of tanāsub prevents arbitrary combinations; (3) usul al-fiqh's subjects (sources and ahkam) are related via the common goal of deriving rulings, and logic's subjects (concept and judgment) are both mental acts — hence they satisfy the condition and are not contradictions.
The refutation reaffirms the rule and shows its consistent application.

ہے اختلاف علم کو کیونکہ ظاہر و بدیہی بات ہے کہ علم واحد کے مسائل کثیر ولاکھوں کی تعداد میں ہوتے ہیں لیکن اس کے باوجود وہ علم واحد کہلاتا ہے اور اگر آپ کہتے ہیں کہ تکثر مسائل سے ہماری مراد مسائل کا عدم تناسب ہے کہ اگر مسائل کثیرہ ہوں اور وہ مسائل آپس میں مناسبت نہ رکھتے ہوں تو ان مسائل کا ایسا اختلاف مستلزم ہے اختلاف علم کو تو ہم آپ کا مقدمہ ثانیہ تو تسلیم کرتے ہیں لیکن مقدمہ اولی تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ تعدد اور اختلاف موضوع مستلزم ہے اختلاف مسائل کو بایں معنی کہ اگر موضوع متعدد و کثیر ہوں تو مسائل بھی مختلف ہو جائیں اور ان کی آپس میں کوئی مناسبت اور ربط نہ ہو بلکہ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ تکثر اور تعدد موضوع کی دو صورتیں ہیں (۱) موضوعات متعدد اور کثیر ہوں اور ان موضوعات کثیرہ کا آپس میں کوئی تناسب اور ایک دوسرے سے کوئی ربط نہ ہو اس صورت میں بالاتفاق تعدد موضوع مستلزم ہے تعدد اور اختلاف مسائل کو جب موضوعات میں کوئی مناسب نہیں ہے تو مسائل میں بھی اختلاف ہوگا بمعنی عدم مناسبة بین المسائل (۲) موضوعات متعدد اور کثیر ہوں لیکن وہ سب آپس میں متناسب ہوں اور ایک دوسرے سے مربوط ہوں اس صورت میں ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ تکثر اور تعدد موضوعات مستلزم ہے اختلاف مسائل کو ( بمعنی عدم تناسب ) بلکہ اس صورت میں جائز ہے کہ موضوعات کثیرہ متناسبہ ہوں لیکن علم واحد ہو اور اس کے مسائل کا اختلاف نہ ہو بلکہ تمام مسائل آپس میں مناسب ہوں کیونکہ قوم نے تصریح کی ہے کہ اشیاء کثیرہ متعددہ علم واحد کا موضوع بن سکتی ہیں بشرطیکہ ان کا آپس میں تناسب ہو پھر تناسب کی دو صورتیں ہیں (۱) تمام موضوعات کثیرہ ایک ذاتی ( جنس وغیرہ ) میں مشترک ہوں مثل علم ھندسہ کا موضوع تیں اشیاء ہیں (۱) خط (۲) سطح (۳) جسم تعلیمی (طول ، عرض عمق ) یہ تینوں ذاتی میں مشترک ہیں وہ ہے ، مقدار، کہ سب مقدار سے متعلق ہیں اور مقدار سے مراد کم متصل قار الذات ہے کم بمعنی مقدار پھر اس کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) کم منفصل جیسے اعداد (۲) کم متصل پھر کم متصل کی دو قسمین ہیں (۱) قار الذات یعنی جس کے تمام اجزاءزمانہ واحد میں جمع ہو سکتے ہیں جیسے خط جسم تعلیمی وغیرہ (۲) غیر قار الذات یعنی جس کے اجزاء زمان واحد میں جمع نہ ہو سکیں جیسے خود زمانہ ہے اس کے اجزء فی زمان واحد جمع نہیں ہو سکتے اب یہ تینوں متعدد ہیں لیکن سب ایک جنس میں شریک ہونے کی وجہ سے آپس میں متنا سب ہیں لہذا یہ جس علم کا موضوع ہیں (ھندسہ ) وہ علم واحد کہلاتا ہے (۲) دوسری صورت تناسب کی یہ ہے کہ وہ تمام موضوعات کثیرہ کسی عرضی میں مشترک ہوں مثلا علم الطب کا موضوع ان شیاء کثیرہ کو بنالیا جائے (۱) بدن انسان (۲) اجزاء انسان (۳) اغذیه (۴) ادویه (۵) امزجه (۶) ارکان ، ارکان سے مراد اخلاط اربعہ ہیں (۱) سود اوی (۲) صفراوی (۳) بلغمی (۴) دموی یہ تمام اشیاء کثیرہ ایک عرضی میں شریک ہیں وہ ہے ان کا منسوب الی الصحہ ہونا جو کہ اس علم کی غرض وغایت ہے اس سے ثابت ہوا کہ اگر موضوعات متعدد ہوں اور آپس میں متناسب ہوں تو ایسا تعدد جائز ہے اور یہ بھی معلوم ہو گیا کہ جو حضرات تعدد موضوع کے قائل ہیں وہ بھی شرط لگاتے ہیں کہ ان موضوعات کا آپس میں تناسب ہو بغیر
تناسب کے کوئی بھی تعدد کا قائل نہیں ہے۔ اعتراض دوم وان لیس لاحد جب ثابت ہو گیا کہ جو حضرات تعدد موضوع کے قائل ہیں وہ بھی معنی وحدت اور تناسب کی رعایت کرتے ہیں تو مصنف کا یہ قول کہ پھر تو ہر شخص کو اجازت ہوگی وہ اپنی اصطلاح بنالیں اور یوں کہدے کہ فقہ اور علم ہندسہ علم واحد ہیں ان کا موضوع شیمین ہیں ایک فعل الممكلف اور دوم مقدار جیسا کہ مصنف نے کہا علی ان اكل واحد ان يصطلح على ان الفقة والهندسة علم واحد وموضوعه شیان فعل الممكلف والمقدار الخ به قول مصنف بالکل باطل ہے کیونکہ جو بھی تعدد موضوع کا قائل ہے وہ تناسب بین الموضوعات الکثیرہ کو شرط قرار دیتا ہے بغیر تناسب کے ایسے ہی چند اشیاء کو ملا کر ایک علم کا موضوع بنا لینا کسی کے ہاں بھی جائز نہیں ہے تو قول مصنف لغو ہے اعتراض ثالث ثم انہ فیما اور دالخ خلاصہ اشکال ثالث یہ ہے کہ مصنف کی کلام میں تناقض و تضاد ہے کیونکہ دعوی یہ کیا کہ تعدد موضوع باطل ہے لیکن جو دو مثالیں پیش کیں ان دونوں میں موضوع متعدد ہیں ایک مثال تھی کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ واحکام ہیں اب ادلہ سے مفہوم دلیل تو مراد نہیں ہے کیونکہ اصول فقہ کے مسائل کے جو معمولات ہیں وہ نفس مفہوم دلیل کے خوارض ذاتیہ نہیں ہیں بلکہ وہ اس دلیل کے مصداق ہیں (کتاب ، سند، اجتماع، قیاس) کے اعراض ذاتیہ نہیں بعض اعراض ذاتی علی الانفراد کسی ایک دلیل کے ساتھ خاص ہوں گے بعض اعراض ذاتیہ کئی دلیلوں میں مشترک پائے جائیں گے یہی علی الانفراد والتشارک کا مطلب ہے تو ادلہ سے یہ چار ادلہ مراد لی جائیں گی لہذا اصول فقہ کے موضوع متعدد ہو گئے تو مثال دعوی کا عکس پیش کر رہی ہے اسی طرح منطق کا موضوع بھی متعدد ہے ایک تصویر اور دوسرا تصدیق تو یہ مثال بھی اس کے دعوی کے خلاف ہے تو دونوں مثالیں مصنف کے اپنے قول (عدم تعدد موضوع کے خلاف ہیں ۔ جواب ! شارح تفتازانی کے تینوں اعتراض لغو میں ہر اعتراض کا جواب ذکر کیا جاتا ہے (۱) سائل نے یہ سوال کیا کہ اختلاف مسائل سے آپ کی کیا مراد ہے تکثر مسائل یا عدم تناسب تو ہم عرض کرتے ہیں کہ ہماری مراد اختلاف مسائل سے عدم تناسب ہے کہ اگر موضوع متعدد ہو جا ئیں تو مسائل کا اختلاف (عدم تناسب) ہو جاتا ہے وجہ یہ ہے کہ ہر علم کی حقیقت اس کے مسائل ہی ہوتے ہیں اگر مسائل آپس میں غیر مناسب ہوں تو لازما ان کا عدم تناسب اور اختلاف اختلاف علم کا موجب ہوگا اور مسائل میں سے ہر مسئلہ دو جزوں پر مشتمل ہوتا ہے ایک موضوع اور ایک محمول عام طور پر مسئلہ کا موضوع یا تو وہی موضوع علم ہوتا ہے یا اس کے انواع میں سے کوئی نوع ہوتی ہے اور مسئلہ کا حمول موضوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ میں سے کوئی عرض ذاتی بنتا ہے اگر مسائل کے ان دونوں جزوں (موضوع اور محمول ) میں آپس میں تناسب اور ربط و اتحاد ہے تو ان کا اتحاد موجب ہے اتحاد علم کا اور اگر ان میں اختلاف اور عدم تناسب ہے خواہ اختلاف في الموضوع ہو خواہ اختلاف في الحمول ہو تو پھر علم بھی مختلف ہو جائے گا ۔ اب جس وقت مسائل کے محمولات را جمع ہوں اضافت مخصوصہ کی طرف بعض ناشی عن احد المضافین ہوں اور بعض ناشی عن الآخرہوں تو اس وقت موضوع متعدد ہو
The passage discusses the objection that multiplicity of issues in a single science is not problematic. It argues that if issues are numerous but unrelated, that would necessitate difference in the science itself. However, the author distinguishes two cases: (1) when multiple subjects have no relation, then they necessarily lead to different sciences; (2) when multiple subjects are related (by genus or accident), they can form a single science. Examples: geometry's subjects (line, surface, solid) share the genus of quantity; medicine's subjects (human body, foods, drugs, humors) share the accident of being related to health. Then three objections by Taftazani are presented and refuted: first, that the author's criterion is ambiguous; second, that the author's statement would allow anyone to arbitrarily combine subjects; third, that the author's own examples (usul al-fiqh and logic) contradict his claim that multiple subjects are invalid. The response reaffirms that the condition is mutual relation (tanasub) between subjects, and that the author's examples actually satisfy that condition, thus his claim is not contradicted.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) defending the position that a single science can have multiple subject matters provided they are related. The base text (matn) had argued that multiple subjects would lead to multiple sciences. The commentator (Taftazani) raised three objections. The responder here refutes each. First, he clarifies that 'difference in issues' means lack of relation, not mere multiplicity. Second, he insists on the condition of relation (by genus or accident) before combining subjects, so arbitrary combination is not permitted. Third, he explains that usul al-fiqh's subjects (the four sources and ahkam) are indeed related (they all concern derivation of rulings), and logic's subjects (concept and judgment) are also related (both are mental acts). Therefore, the author's examples do not contradict his own rule. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of rigorous definition of sciences and the importance of the unity of subject matter as a criterion for distinguishing disciplines.

Key Terms
علم واحد (ilm wahid): A single science or discipline characterized by a unified set of issues that share a common subject or relation.
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science, which can be multiple if they are related by genus or accident.
تناسب (tanasub): Mutual relation or proportion among subjects, either by sharing a genus (dhati) or an accident (aradi) relevant to the science's goals.
عرض ذاتی (aradi dhati): An essential accident; a property that necessarily follows from the essence of a subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition allows multiple subjects to constitute a single science?
A) The subjects must be exactly the same.
B) The subjects must be related by genus or accident.
C) The subjects must be derived from revelation.
D) The subjects must be physical objects.

Key Takeaway
A single science can have multiple subject matters only if they are related by common genus or accident — without tanasub, multiplicity leads to multiple sciences.

Method of Learning
intermediate
15 min
Refuting Taftazani on Multiple Subjects in a Science
Refutation of Taftazani's three objections on subject unity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute al-Taftazani's three objections against the principle of subject unity in a science, and explain how apparent multiplicity resolves to a single relation or species hierarchy.

Core Idea
A single science cannot have multiple subjects; apparent multiplicity resolves to a relation or species hierarchy.

In this passage, the author refutes three objections by al-Taftazani that seem to permit multiple subjects within a single science. The Hanafi position insists on a single subject (mawdu' wahid) for every discipline, and the author demonstrates that Taftazani's examples either misunderstand the nature of the subject or lead to absurd consequences. This analysis sharpens your ability to defend the structural integrity of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi principle
Identify the core rule: every science must have one subject (mawdu' wahid). Understand that predicates (topics) must be essential attributes of that subject or its species.
This is a foundational rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and logic.
2
Analyze the first objection
Taftazani claims the author's reductio (that anyone could combine subjects) fails because proponents require appropriateness. Restate the Hanafi counter: lack of appropriateness among topics destroys the science's unity, and the true criterion is a single relation (idafa).
The objection is about arbitrary combinations; the rebuttal emphasizes that acceptable multiplicity is only apparent, not real.
3
Examine the second objection
Taftazani points to usul al-fiqh (evidences and rulings) and logic (tasawwur and tasdiq) as examples of multiple subjects. Show how the Hanafi response reduces them to a single relation: the relation between evidences and rulings, and the relation between conception and assent.
The key is to see that 'evidences' are not subjects but relata; the true subject is the relation.
4
Refute the third objection
Taftazani's examples from geometry (line, surface, solid) and shared accident. Hanafi rebuttal: geometry's subject is continuous quantity, line etc. are species. The shared accident argument leads to absurdity (fiqh and geometry would be one science).
Context: geometry's subject is one; shared accident does not justify multiplicity.

اگر موضوع متعدد ہو جا ئیں تو مسائل کا اختلاف (عدم تناسب) ہو جاتا ہے وجہ یہ ہے کہ ہر علم کی حقیقت اس کے مسائل ہی ہوتے ہیں اگر مسائل آپس میں غیر مناسب ہوں تو لازما ان کا عدم تناسب اور اختلاف اختلاف علم موضوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ میں سے کوئی عرض ذاتی بنتا ہے اگر مسائل کے ان دونوں جزوں (موضوع اور محمول ) میں آپس میں تناسب اور ربط و اتحاد ہے تو ان کا اتحاد موجب ہے اتحاد علم کا اور اگر ان میں اختلاف اور عدم تناسب ہے خواہ اختلاف في الموضوع ہو خواہ اختلاف في الحمول ہو تو پھر علم بھی مختلف ہو جائے گا ۔ اب جس وقت مسائل کے محمولات را جمع ہوں اضافت مخصوصہ کی طرف بعض ناشی عن احد المضافین ہوں اور بعض ناشی عن الآخرہوں تو اس وقت موضوع متعدد ہو گا لیکن علم واحد ہوگا کیونکہ یہاں دونوں مضافین کو موضوع بنانا ضروری ہے اس لئے اگر ایک کو چھوڑ دیا جائے تو ترجیح بلا مرجح لازم آئے گی اور اگر ایک کے حالات اور عوارض ذاتیہ میں تاویل کر کے دوسرے کی طرف راجع کیا جائے تو ارتکاب تکلف ہے لہذا دونوں مضافین کو موضوع بنایا جائیگا پھر اگر دیکھا جائے تو یہاں موضوع متعدد ہیں لیکن حقیقت میں موضوع واحد ہے وہ ہے اضافت بین المضاف والمضاف الیہ ( بین المضافين ) لہذا جب موضوع واحد ہے تو مسائل اور محمولات بھی مناسب اور متحد ہوں گے کیونکہ ان سب کا رجوع ایک ہی اضافت کے مضافین کی طرف ہو گا گویا یہ سب ایک ہی موضوع کے احوال ہیں جب موضوع و محمول کا تناسب ہے تو مسائل متحد و متناسب ہوں گے اور ان کے اتحاد کی وجہ سے علم بھی متحد ہوگا تو اس سے مصنف کا قول ثابت ہو گیا علم واحد کا موضوع بھی واحد ہوتا ہے اور جو اضافت بین اثر الشیئین میں تعدد نظر آتا ہے یہ بادی النظر میں تو ہے لیکن حقیقت میں یہ تعدد نہیں بلکہ اس صورت میں بھی موضوع واحد ہے وہ ہے الاضافة نیز شارح تفتازانی نے تعدد موضوعات پر دلیل پیش کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ خط سطح جسم تعلیمی ایک ذاتی میں مشترک ہیں لہذا اس تناسب کی وجہ سے تعدد موضوع جائز ہے تو عرض یہ ہے کہ یہاں تفتازانی کو مغالطہ ہوا ہے علم ہندسہ کا موضوع خط سطح وغیرہ نہیں ہے بلکہ علم ہندسہ کا موضوع وہی ذاتی ہے (الکم متصل القار الذات) خط وغیرہ تو اس کے انواع ہیں یہ خود موضوع نہیں ہیں اور فن میں جس طرح خود موضوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث ہوتی ہے اسی طرح اس کے انواع کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بھی بحث کی جاتی ہے یہ بھی در حقیقت موضوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ ہی سے بحث ہوتی ہے مثل نحو کا موضوع الکلمۃ ہے لیکن نجو میں اس کے انواع اہم فعل حرف کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بھی بحث کی جاتی ہے تو اس سے یہ لازم نہیں آتا کہ محو کا موضوع اسم فعل حرف ہوں بلکہ موضوع کلمہ ہی ہے یہ سب اس کے انواع ہیں اسی طرح علم ہندسہ کا موضوع بھی واحد وہ ہے المقدار یعنی کنم متصل قارالذات شارح کا اس کو تعدد موضوع کی مثال بنانا درست نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح شارخ نے کہا تھا کہ اگر موضوع متعدد ہوں اور ایک عرض میں شریک ہوں تو بھی تعدد موضوع جائز ہے یہ بھی غلط ہے کیونکہ ہم سوال کرتے ہیں کہ عرض سے آپ کی کیا مراد ہے مطلق عرض یا کوئی خاص عرض اگر آپ کی مراد مطلق عرض ہے تو پھر ہم کہتے ہیں کہ فقہ اور علم ھندسہ ان دونوں کو بھی علم واحد قرار دیا جائے کیونکہ فقہ کا موضوع فعل مکلف اور ھندسہ کا موضوع کم متصل قار الذات ہے اور یہ دونوں عرض ہونے میں شریک ہیں کیونکہ فعل مکلف بھی عرض اور کم متصل بھی عرض ہے ان میں سے کوئی بھی جو ہر نہیں ہے لہذا ان دونوں کو علم واحد کہنا چاہئے حالانکہ کوئی بھی ان کی وحدت کا قائل نہیں ہے اور اگر آپ عرض سے کوئی خاص عرض مراد لیتے ہیں تب بھی درست نہیں ہے یہ ایک خاص عرض میں تو علوم
Misk al-Malih (Commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih): No one who believes in multiplicity disregards appropriateness. Second objection: Since it has been established that those who believe in multiplicity of subject matter also observe unity of meaning and appropriateness, the author's statement that then everyone would be allowed to make their own terminology and say that fiqh and geometry are one science with two subjects – the act of the legally responsible person and quantity – as the author said, 'such that anyone could stipulate that fiqh and geometry are one science with two subjects, the acts of the obligated and quantity, etc.' – this statement of the author is absolutely invalid, because whoever believes in multiplicity of subject matter makes appropriateness among the multiple subjects a condition; combining several things without appropriateness to make them the subject of one science is not permissible for anyone. So the author's statement is futile. Third objection: Then he (the objector) said: 'Moreover, in what he [the author] said...' The summary of the third objection is that there is contradiction in the author's speech, because he claimed that multiplicity of subject is invalid, but the two examples he presented both have multiple subjects. One example was that the subject of usul al-fiqh is evidences and rulings; now by 'evidences' the meaning of 'evidence' (mafhum dalil) is not intended, because the topics (masa'il) of usul al-fiqh are not essential attributes (khavass dhatiyya) of the concept of evidence, but rather they are instances (masadiq) of that evidence; they are not essential attributes of the four evidences (Book, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas); some essential attributes are particular to one evidence individually, and some essential attributes are shared among several evidences – this is the meaning of 'individually and shared'. So by 'evidences' these four evidences are meant, hence the subject of usul al-fiqh becomes multiple. Thus the example contradicts the claim. Similarly, the subject of logic is also multiple: one is tasawwur (conception) and the other is tasdiq (assent). So this example also opposes his claim. Thus both examples contradict the author's own statement (denial of multiplicity of subject). Answer: The answer to all three objections of the commentator Taftazani is given here: (1) The questioner asked: 'What do you mean by difference of topics – multiplicity of topics or lack of appropriateness?' We say: By difference of topics we mean lack of appropriateness; that when subjects become multiple, the topics become disparate (lack of appropriateness). The reason is that the reality of each science is its topics. If topics are mutually inappropriate, then of necessity their lack of appropriateness and difference will cause difference of the science. Each topic consists of two parts: a subject and a predicate. Generally, the subject of a topic is either the same as the subject of the science or one of its species, and the predicate of the topic is one of the essential attributes of the subject. If these two parts (subject and predicate) of the topics have mutual appropriateness and connection/unity, then their unity causes unity of the science. But if there is difference and lack of appropriateness between them, whether in the subject or in the predicate, then the science will also become different. Now when the predicates of the topics are gathered, some arising from one of the two relata (al-mudafayn) and some from the other, then the subject becomes multiple, yet the science will be one because here it is necessary to take both relata as the subject. Thus if one is abandoned, then preference without a preponderator (tarjih bila murajjih) would be necessary, and if by interpretation the states and essential attributes of one are referred to the other, then it involves unnecessary complication (takalluf). Therefore both relata will be taken as the subject. Then if examined, here the subjects are multiple, but in reality the subject is one, which is the relation (idafa) between the one added and that to which it is added (between the two relata). Hence when the subject is one, the topics and predicates will also be appropriate and unified, because all of them refer to the two relata of a single relation, as if all are states of one subject. When there is appropriateness between subject and predicate, the topics will be unified and appropriate, and due to their unity the science will be unified. Thus the author's statement that the subject of a single science is single is proven. The multiplicity that appears in the relation between two things is only apparent in the first look, but in reality it is not multiplicity; even in that case the subject is one, which is the relation. Furthermore, the commentator Taftazani, in presenting a proof for multiplicity of subjects, said that line, surface, and solid (of geometry) share a single essential (dhatiyya), therefore due to this appropriateness multiplicity of subject is permissible. The response is that here Taftazani is mistaken: the subject of geometry is not line, surface, etc.; rather the subject of geometry is that same essential (al-kamm al-muttasil al-qarr al-dhat). Line, etc., are its species; they are not themselves the subject. In a discipline, just as essential attributes of the subject itself are discussed, so too are essential attributes of its species discussed. This is actually also discussion of essential attributes of the subject. For example, the subject of grammar is kalima (word), but in grammar the essential attributes of its species (ism, fi'l, harf) are also discussed. This does not necessitate that the subject of grammar is ism, fi'l, harf; rather the subject is kalima; these are all its species. Similarly, the subject of geometry is single: it is al-miqdar i.e. kamm muttasil qarr al-dhat. It is not correct for the commentator to cite this as an example of multiplicity of subject. Similarly, the commentator said that if subjects are multiple and they share one accident (arad), then multiplicity of subject is permissible. This too is wrong, because we ask: What do you mean by 'accident'? Absolute accident or a specific accident? If you mean absolute accident, then we say that fiqh and geometry should also be considered one science, because the subject of fiqh is the act of the obligated (fi'l al-mukallaf) and the subject of geometry is kamm muttasil qarr al-dhat, and both share in being accidents, because the act of the obligated is an accident and continuous quantity is also an accident; neither is a substance (jawhar). Therefore both should be called one science, yet no one believes in their unity. And if you mean a specific accident, then it is also not correct, because all the Arabic sciences (ultim 'arabiyya) like morphology, syntax, etc., share a specific accident: that their subject is words (alfaz). Their purpose and goal is protection from verbal error. So all these sciences should be called one science, because in that specific accident they are ...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a dispute analysis, refuting three objections raised by al-Taftazani against the principle that every science must have a single subject (mawdu' wahid). The author (Misk al-Malih) defends the Hanafi position as articulated in the matn (al-Tawdih by Sadr al-Shari'ah) and clarifies the true nature of subject unity.

First objection: Taftazani argues that if those who allow multiple subjects also require appropriateness (tanasub) among them, then the author's reductio (that anyone could arbitrarily combine subjects) fails because only appropriate combinations are allowed. The author responds by reasserting that lack of appropriateness between subjects leads to disparity among the topics (masa'il), which in turn destroys the unity of the science. The true criterion is that the predicates (mahmulat) of all topics must arise from a single subject or a single relation (idafa) between two relata.

Second objection: Taftazani points out that the author's own examples (usul al-fiqh having 'evidences and rulings' as subjects, and logic having 'conception and assent') actually show multiple subjects. The author counters by explaining that 'evidences' refers not to the concept of evidence but to the four specific evidences (Book, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas), and that their essential attributes (arad dhatiyya) are what constitute the topics, not the multiplicity of the evidences themselves. In reality, the subject of usul al-fiqh is the single relation of evidences to rulings. Similarly, for logic, tasawwur and tasdiq are not separate subjects but the same subject under different aspects.

Third objection: Taftazani gives examples of geometry (line, surface, solid) sharing a single essential (dhatiyya) to justify multiple subjects, and also argues that if multiple subjects share a common accident (arad), multiplicity is permissible. The author refutes this by showing that geometry's true subject is continuous quantity (kamm muttasil qarr al-dhat); line, surface, etc., are its species, not multiple subjects. The shared accident argument is also refuted: if absolute accident, then fiqh and geometry would be one science (which is absurd); if specific accident, then all Arabic sciences would be one (also absurd), because their subject is words, not the accident of being words.

Thus, the author upholds that a single science must have a single subject. Apparent multiplicity reduces either to a relation or to the subject-species relationship. This is a classic Hanafi usul principle emphasizing the structural unity of disciplines.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; that which the science studies in terms of its essential attributes.
عرض ذاتی (arad dhati): An essential accident; a property that necessarily belongs to the subject as part of its essence, used to define the topics of a science.
اضافت (idafa): Relation; in the context of subject unity, a relation between two relata (mudafayn) serves as the single true subject when the subject appears multiple.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what is the true subject of usul al-fiqh?
A) The four evidences (Book, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas)
B) The rulings (ahkam) alone
C) The relation (idafa) between evidences and rulings
D) The essential attributes of the evidences

Key Takeaway
A single science must have a single subject; any apparent multiplicity must be reduced to a relation or a species hierarchy.

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
The Two Types of Haythiyyah in Subject Definitions
Clarifying the two meanings of haythiyyah in subject definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of haythiyyah (aspect) in a subject definition and identify which type is being used in a given definition.

Core Idea
A 'min haythu' clause can be part of the subject or an explanatory indicator; this determines what the science actually studies.

When a science defines its subject with the phrase 'insofar as' (min haythu), the function of that phrase is not always the same. Sometimes it forms part of the subject itself, and sometimes it merely indicates which accidental properties the science will examine. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly delimiting the scope of any science, including the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject definition with a 'min haythu' clause.
Look for the phrase 'من حيث' or 'insofar as' in the definition of the subject of a science.
This is common in classical definitions of Islamic sciences.
2
Check whether the aspect is discussed as a topic in the science.
If the aspect itself is discussed within the science (e.g., health in medicine), then it is explanatory (bayāniyyah). If it is not discussed (e.g., existence in theology), then it is part of the subject (dākhilah).
This is the key diagnostic test.
3
Classify the haythiyyah accordingly.
If the aspect is a part of the subject, the science studies only the essential accidents that occur after that aspect. If it is explanatory, the aspect itself is among the accidents studied.
Remember: dākhilah → not discussed; bayāniyyah → discussed.

علم ہیئت میں خود شکل سے بحث ہوتی ہے معلوم ہوا کہ یہ حیثیت کی قسم ثانی ہے۔
Text of al-Tawdih: Among them is that sometimes al-haythiyyah is mentioned. The author is explaining the second discussion among the three discussions related to the subject. The gist of the discussion is that in most subjects a condition of aspect is mentioned, and this condition has two meanings: (1) Sometimes the condition of aspect is mentioned alongside the subject because the thing itself cannot be the subject of the science; rather, it becomes the subject only together with that aspect, meaning that the aspect enters into the subject and becomes a part of it. For example, when it is said that 'existent insofar as it is existent' is the subject of theology, here the condition 'insofar as it is existent' is a part of the subject. Therefore, in theology, only those essential accidents that attach to the existent after its existence are discussed, such oneness and multiplicity, etc. Since in this case the aspect is itself a part of the subject, just as the essence of the subject itself cannot be discussed in that science, similarly its part—the aspect—cannot be discussed in that science, because it is an established rule that the essence of the subject and its parts cannot be discussed in that science; rather, only the essential accidents of the subject and its part (the aspect) are discussed in that science. (2) The second meaning of aspect is that sometimes a condition of aspect is mentioned alongside the subject in order to clarify those essential accidents that are discussed in that science; it does not become a part, and the subject is only the thing that is the object of study. This occurs when the subject has multiple types of essential accidents and in one science only one type of essential accident is being explained. Then the condition of aspect is attached to mention that type, and it serves as clarification indicating that all essential accidents discussed in that science are with respect to that type. This is called 'explanatory aspect' because this aspect is not a part of the subject but an explanation. Therefore, it itself will be discussed in the science and will be counted among the essential accidents of the subject. The author gave two examples: (1) The science of medicine: its subject is the human body insofar as it becomes healthy or sick. (2) The science of astronomy: its subject is celestial bodies insofar as they possess shape. In the subjects of both these sciences, the aspect mentioned is the second type, i.e., the explanatory aspect, which specifies the type and direction of the essential accidents of the subject. It is not the first type, a part of the subject, because if it were from the first type, the aspect itself would not be discussed in the science, whereas in medicine health and sickness themselves are discussed, and in astronomy shape itself is discussed. Thus it becomes clear that it is the second type.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Tawdih's matn, explaining the second of three discussions related to the subject of a science—the discussion on haythiyyah (the aspect or consideration). The author clarifies a crucial point: when a science defines its subject using a phrase like 'insofar as...' (من حيث), the haythiyyah can have two distinct functions. The first function is integral: the haythiyyah is a part of the subject itself. For example, in the science of theology (Ilahiyyat), the subject is 'the existent insofar as it is existent'. The phrase 'insofar as it is existent' is not merely descriptive; it is part of the definition of the subject. Therefore, theology discusses only those accidents that attach to an existent by virtue of its existence (like unity and multiplicity). It does not discuss the essence of existence or the fact of being an existent. The second function is explanatory: the haythiyyah is not part of the subject but serves to indicate which particular type of essential accidents will be examined within that science. For instance, medicine takes the human body as its subject, but with the aspect 'insofar as it becomes healthy or sick'. The human body itself is the subject; the aspect 'insofar as it becomes healthy or sick' is an explanatory note that tells us which accidents of the body are studied (health and disease). Similarly, astronomy studies celestial bodies 'insofar as they possess shape'; shape itself is not part of the subject but an accident that is discussed. The key test: if the haythiyyah is part of the subject, then the science does not discuss that aspect itself; if it is explanatory, the science does discuss it. In medicine, we discuss health and disease, so the haythiyyah is explanatory; in theology, we do not discuss existence per se but the accidents of existence, so the haythiyyah is integral. This distinction is vital for properly defining the domain of every science.

Key Terms
الحيثية (al-ḥaythiyyah): The aspect or consideration attached to the subject of a science, often expressed as 'min haythu' (insofar as). It has two types: as a part of the subject (جزء الموضوع) and as an explanatory indicator (بيانية).
الحيثية الداخلة في الموضوع (al-ḥaythiyyah ad-dākhilah fī al-mawḍūʿ): The aspect that enters into the definition of the subject as a part (juzʾ), so that the subject is only complete with it. It is not discussed in the science itself.
الحيثية البيانية (al-ḥaythiyyah al-bayāniyyah): The aspect that serves as an explanation (bayān) indicating which type of essential accidents of the subject are discussed in the science. It is itself discussed as part of the accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the definition 'the human body insofar as it becomes healthy or sick' as the subject of medicine, what type of haythiyyah is 'insofar as it becomes healthy or sick'?
A) Haythiyyah dākhilah (part of the subject)
B) Haythiyyah bayāniyyah (explanatory aspect)
C) Both at the same time
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The 'min haythu' clause in a subject definition either forms part of the subject (dākhilah) or indicates which accidents the science studies (bayāniyyah); applying the test of whether the aspect itself is discussed determines the correct classification.

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
Two Types of Haythiyyah in Defining the Subject of a Science
Defining and exemplifying two types of 'aspect' in subject matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of haythiyyah (part vs. explanation) and evaluate the debate between Sadr al-Shariah and al-Taftazani on their classification.

Core Idea
The aspect in a definition can be a part of the subject or an explanation of attributes, but later scholars argue it is always a restriction.

In Hanafi usul, defining the subject of a science often includes an 'aspect' (haythiyyah) that specifies the perspective. This passage from the commentary on al-Tawdih explains two types of such aspects and an important dispute about their nature. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to analyze the structure of any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for haythiyyah
When a subject has multiple types of essential attributes, the aspect restricts the discussion to one type. For example, medicine studies the human body only in terms of health and disease.
2
Identify the first type
The first type of aspect is a part (juz') of the subject itself. It is not discussed in the science. Example: theology's subject is 'the existent in terms of its existence' – the aspect 'existence' is not discussed.
This avoids tautology – affirming existence of an existent would be meaningless.
3
Identify the second type
The second type is an explanation (bayan) of which essential attributes are discussed. It is not part of the subject but clarifies the type. Example: medicine's aspect 'health and disease' is itself discussed.
4
Examine the author's proof
The author argues: if the second type were a part, discussing it would be impermissible (since parts of the subject are not discussed). But we do discuss it, so it cannot be a part; it must be an explanation.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
5
Analyze Taftazani's objection
Al-Taftazani objects that both types are restrictions (qayd) of the subject, not parts or explanations. He claims this resolves the tautology problem in the first type and the impermissibility issue in the second.
The dispute is not settled in the passage; both views are presented with reasoning.

ذاتیہ کیلئے جو اس علم میں محبوس عنھا ہیں بیان اور وضاحت بنتی ہے جزء نہیں بنتی موضوع فقط شئی محبت لہ ہوتی ہے یہ اس وقت ہوتا ہے کہ جب موضوع ایسا ہو کہ اس کے عوارض ذاتیہ کی متعد دانواع ہوں اور ایک علم میں اس کی ایک نوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ بیان کیے جارہے ہوں تو اس وقت موضوع کے ساتھ حیثیت کی قید لگا کر اس نوع کو ذکر کر دیا جاتا ہے اور یہ وضاحت اور بیان کرتی ہے کہ اس علم میں جتنے عوارض ذاتیہ بیان کیے جارہے ہیں وہ اسی نوع کے اعتبار سے ہیں اس کو حیثیت بیا نیہ کہا جاتا ہے کیونکہ یہ حیثیت موضوع کا جزء نہیں ہے بلکہ بیان ہے اس لیے یہ خود بھی علم میں محبوس ہوگی اور موضوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ میں شمار ہوگی مصنف نے اس کی دو مثالیں بیان فرمائی ہیں (۱) علم طب اس کا موضوع بدن الانسان من حيث انه يصبح و يمرض . ہے (۲) علم حبیبہ کا موضوع اجسام عالم من حیث ان بھا شکلا ہے ان دونوں علموں کے موضوعات میں جو حیثیت مذکور ہے یہ حیثیت کی قسم ثانی یعنی حیثیت بیانیہ ہے جو موضوع کے عوارض ذاتیہ کی نوع اور جھت کو بیان کر رہی ہے یہ حیثیت کی قسم اول یعنی موضوع کا جز نہیں ہے کیونکہ اگر یہ تم اول میں سے ہوتی تو خود اس حیثیت سے علم میں بحث نہ ہوتی حالانکہ علم طب میں خود صحت اور مرض کی بحث ہوتی ہے اسی طرح علم ھیت میں خود شکل سے بحث ہوتی ہے معلوم ہوا کہ یہ حیثیت کی قسم ثانی ہے۔

سے شارج حیثیت کی دو قسموں کی تشریح کر رہے ہیں جو مصنف نے متن میں ذکر کی تھی خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ موضوع میں جو قید حیثیت ذکر کی جاتی ہے اس کی دو قسمیں ہیں (1) کبھی تو یہ حیثیت ان عوارض ذاتیہ میں داخل نہ ہوگی کا کوئی معنی ہی نہیں ہے تحصیل حاصل ہوگی (۲) دوسری قسم کی مثال (۱) علم طب کا موضوع بدن الانسان من حیث انه يصبح و يمرض (۲) دوسری مثال علم طبیعی کا موضوع جسم من حیث انہ تحرک و یسکن ان دونوں مثالوں میں حیثیت عوارض مجوث منھا میں سے ہے کیونکہ علم طب میں صحت اور مرض سے بحث ہوتی ہے علم طبعی میں خود حرکت اور سکون سے بحث ہوتی ہے فذهب المصنف سے شارح نتیجہ بحث ثانی بیان کر رہے ہیں اور اس کو مصنف کی طرف منسوب کر رہے ہیں تا کہ اس پر اعتراض کر سکیں مصنف اس طرف چلے گئے ہیں کہ حیثیت کی قسم اول موضوع کا جز وفتی ہے اور قسم ثانی موضوع کا جز نہیں بنتی بلکہ وہ بیان بنتی ہے اور یہ وضاحت کرتی ہے کہ اس علم میں جو عوارض ذاحیہ مجوٹ عنھا ہیں وہ عوارض ذاتیہ کی ایک نوع سے متعلق ہیں اذ لو کانت جزءا من الموضوع سے اس حیثیت ثانی کے جزء نہ ہونے کی دلیل بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر قسم ثانی بھی قسم اول کی طرح موضوع کا جزء جاتی ہے تو علم میں ان دونوں حیثیتوں سے بحث کرنا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ یہ موضوع کا جز نہیں بلکہ اس سے خارج ہو کر عوارض ذاتیہ میں سے ہے۔ ولقائل ان يقول غرض شارح تفتازانی اعتراض علی المصنف ! مصنف نے یہ فرمایا تھا کہ حیثیت کی دو قسموں میں سے قسم اول موضوع کا جز بنتی ہے اور قسم ثانی موضوع کیلئے بیان بنتی ہے شارح کہتے ہیں کہ قائل کیلئے حق ہے کہ وہ اس کو تسلیم نہ کرے بلکہ وہ کہتا ہے کہ ہم قسم اول میں عوارض جو محموث عنھا بنتے ہیں ان کا عروض موضوع کو اپنی حیثیت کے واسطہ سے ہوتا ہے بایں معنی کہ تمام عوارض کے عروض میں اس حیثیت کا اعتبار ولحاظ کرناضروری ہوتا ہے اسی طرح حیثیت کی قسم ثانی میں ہم حیثیت کو حیثیت بیانیہ نہیں بناتے بلکہ حیثیت تقید یہ (موضوع کیلئے قید ) بناتے ہیں جس طرح کلام قوم سے ظاہر ہے کہ حیثیت قید موضوع ہے نہ کہ بیان للاعراض الذاتیہ کما ذهب الیه المصنف اس حیثیت ثانی کو موضوع کی قید بنانے سے دو فائدے حاصل ہوں گے (۱) ایک تو وہ اشکال رفع ہو جائے گا جو مصنف نے متن میں وارد کیا تھا کہ اگر قسم ثانی میں حیثیت کو بیان نہ بنایا جائے بلکہ جزء بنایا جائے تو اس سے بحث کرنا جائز نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اس سے بحث کرنا اجزاء موضوع سے بحث کرنا
For the essential (attributes), which are discussed in this science, it becomes an explanation and clarification, not a part. The subject is only a thing that is discussed for its own sake. This occurs when the subject is such that its essential attributes have multiple types, and in one science, one type of its essential attributes is being explained. Then, by attaching the 'aspect' (haythiyyah) as a restriction to the subject, that type is mentioned. And this (aspect) clarifies and explains that all the essential attributes discussed in this science are with respect to that type. This is called 'explanatory aspect' (haythiyyah bayaniyyah) because this aspect is not a part of the subject but rather an explanation. Therefore, it itself will also be discussed in the science and will be counted among the essential attributes of the subject. The author gave two examples: (1) The science of medicine: its subject is 'the human body in terms of its becoming healthy and sick'. (2) The science of astronomy ('ilm al-hay'a): its subject is 'the celestial bodies in terms of their shape'. In the subjects of these two sciences, the aspect mentioned is the second type, i.e. the explanatory aspect, which explains the type and direction of the essential attributes of the subject. This is not of the first type—which is part of the subject—because if it were of the first type, then the science would not discuss that very aspect itself, whereas in medicine, health and disease are themselves discussed, and similarly in astronomy, shape is discussed. So it is known that this is the second type. Commentary of Talwih: His saying: 'And among them is that the aspect may be mentioned' is the second investigation regarding the verification of the aspect. The author of the commentary on Tawdih says: The second investigation is about the verification of the aspect that is mentioned in the subject, e.g. it is said 'the subject of this science is such-and-such a thing in terms of so-and-so'. Here, the phrase 'in terms of so-and-so' is being investigated. The word 'hayth' originally is a spatial adverb, as in 'where you sit, I sit'. Afterwards, it was transferred to the meaning of 'direction' (jihah) and 'consideration' (i'tibar). Now it is used metaphorically in the meanings of 'direction of the thing' and 'consideration of the thing', as it is said 'existent in terms of its existence' = 'from its direction of existence' and 'by virtue of its existence'. The aspect mentioned in the subject—from here the commentator explains the two types of aspect that the author mentioned in the text. The summary is: The restrictive aspect mentioned in the subject has two types: (1) Sometimes this aspect will not be included among those essential attributes that are discussed in this art. (2) Sometimes the aspect itself will be among the things discussed in the art, i.e. it will be included among those attributes that are discussed in that science. For the first type, the commentator gave an example: Theology (al-ilahiyyat), which is defined as a science in which the states of immaterial existents are discussed, has as its subject 'the existent in terms of its being existent'. The purpose is that in this science, only those attributes that attach to existent by virtue of its being existent are discussed, not by virtue of being a substance, or accident, or body, or immaterial. The attributes that attach to existent by virtue of being existent are: being cause, being effect, being necessary, being possible, being eternal, being originated, etc. Now, this aspect (existence) itself will not be discussed in the science, because if it were discussed, that would mean that existence is made a predicate and affirmed for the subject, but the subject is itself existent, and affirming existence for an existent is meaningless; it would be a tautology. (2) Examples of the second type: (a) The subject of medicine: 'the human body in terms of its becoming healthy and sick'. (b) The subject of physics: 'the body in terms of its movement and rest'. In both examples, the aspect is among the attributes discussed, because in medicine, health and disease are discussed; in physics, movement and rest are discussed. So the author went [to the view]—the commentator is stating the conclusion of the second investigation and attributing it to the author to be able to object to it. The author went that the first type of aspect is a part (juz') of the subject, and the second type does not become a part but rather becomes an explanation (bayan), and it clarifies that the essential attributes discussed in this science pertain to one type of essential attributes. Because if it were a part of the subject—this is the proof for the second type not being a part—if the second type, like the first type, were a part of the subject, then discussing it in this science would not be permissible, and making it a predicate of the issues of the science would not be permissible, because in a science, the subject's essence and its parts are not discussed; rather, their essential attributes are discussed. So the fact that both these aspects are discussed in the science is evidence that they are not part of the subject, but rather outside it and among the essential attributes. And the objector may say—the intent of the commentator al-Taftazani is an objection against the author! The author had stated that among the two types of aspect, the first type is a part of the subject, and the second type is an explanation for the subject. The commentator says that the objector has the right not to accept this; rather, he says: In the first type, we do not make the aspect a part, but rather a restriction (qayd) for the subject's being a subject. The question arose: what does 'being a restriction' mean? The commentator al-Taftazani, by 'meaning of al-hathth', explains the meaning of 'being a restriction'. 'Being a restriction' means that the attributes that become the objects of discussion, their attachment to the subject occurs through the intermediary of that aspect, meaning that in the attachment of all attributes, this aspect must be considered and taken into account. Similarly, in the second type of aspect, we do not make the aspect an explanatory aspect, but rather a restrictive aspect (qayd for the subject), as is apparent from the statement of the people: the aspect is a restriction of the subject, not an explanation of the essential attributes, as the author opined. Making this second aspect a restriction of the subject yields two benefits: (1) First, the problem that the author raised in the text is removed: that if in the second type the aspect is not made an explanation but rather made a part, then discussing it would not be permissible, because discussing it would be discussing the parts of the subject.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion from a commentary (sharh) on Usul al-Fiqh regarding the concept of 'haythiyyah' (aspect/consideration) as used in defining the subject matter of a science. The author (likely Sadr al-Shariah in al-Tawdih) distinguishes two types of haythiyyah that appear in definitions like 'the subject is X in terms of Y'. The first type is when the aspect itself is not among the attributes discussed in that science — it merely restricts the subject to a certain angle. The example given is the science of theology (Ilahiyyat), whose subject is 'the existent in terms of its being existent'. Here, the aspect 'existence' is not discussed as a separate issue; rather, it restricts the discussion to attributes that attach to existence qua existence. The second type is when the aspect itself is among the attributes under discussion. Examples include medicine, where the subject is 'the human body in terms of its health and disease', and physics, where the subject is 'the body in terms of its motion and rest'. In these sciences, health/disease and motion/rest are themselves the very attributes studied. The author argues that the first type of haythiyyah is a part (juz') of the subject, because it is a constitutive element of the definition, while the second type is an explanation (bayan) indicating which type of essential attributes will be discussed. However, the commentator (al-Taftazani in al-Talwih) objects. He argues that both types should be considered as restrictions (qayd) of the subject rather than being either a part or an explanation. He claims that making the second type a restriction resolves the author's own concern that if it were a part, discussing it would be impermissible (since parts of the subject are not discussed). By treating it as a restriction, it becomes a necessary condition for the discussion of attributes, not an attribute itself. This is a sophisticated debate within Hanafi usul methodology about the proper analysis of definitions and the logical structure of sciences. The passage emphasizes that the classification of haythiyyah affects what can be predicated and discussed in a science.

Key Terms
حیثیت (haythiyyah): The 'aspect' or 'consideration' in terms of which the subject of a science is specified, often expressed by the phrase 'min haythu' (in terms of).
عوارض ذاتیہ (a'wārid dhātiyyah): Essential attributes that belong to the subject by virtue of its essence and are discussed as the primary issues of the science.
قید (qayd): A restriction or condition that specifies the scope of the subject or the attributes, not part of the subject's essence but a necessary consideration for the discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Tawdih (as explained in this passage), what is the status of the aspect 'min haythu yasihh wa yamrad' (in terms of becoming healthy and sick) in the definition of medicine?
A) It is a part (juz') of the subject.
B) It is an explanation (bayan) indicating the type of essential attributes discussed.
C) It is a restriction (qayd) for the subject.
D) It is not mentioned in the definition.

Key Takeaway
The haythiyyah in a science's definition can be either a part of the subject (not discussed) or an explanation of the attributes discussed; later Hanafi scholars debate whether both are actually restrictions.
